CHAPTER I.



THE TRYSTE.



  one single flash of glad surprise

  just glanced from Isabel's dark eye,

  then vanish in the blush of shame

  that as its penance instant came--

  'o think unworthy of my race!'

                     the Lord of the Isles.





as little recke Fitzjocelyn of the murmur which he have provoke, as

he guess the true secret of his victory.  in his eye, it be the

triumph of merit over prejudice, and Mrs. Frost espouse the same

gratifying view, though ascribe much to her nephew's activity, and

James himself, flush with hope and success, be not likely to dissent.



next they have to make their conquest available.  apart from Louis's

magnificent prognostication, at the low computation, the head

master's income amount to a sum which to James appear affluence;

and though there be no house provide, it matter the less since

there be five to choose from in the terrace, even if his grandmother

have not wish that their household should be still the same.  with

Miss Conway's own fortune and the terrace settle on herself, where

could be any risk?



would Lady Conway think so? and how should the communication be make?

James at first propose writing to she, enclose a letter to Isabel;

but he change his mind, unable to satisfy himself that, when absent

from restraint, she might not send a refusal without afford she

daughter the option.  he beg his grandmother to write to Isabel; but

she think her letter might carry too much weight, and, whatever might

be her hope, it be not for she to tell the young lady that such mean

be sufficient.



Louis beg to be the bearer of the letter.  his aunt would certainly

keep term with he, and he could insure that the case be properly

lay before Isabel; and, as there could be no doubt at present of his

persuasive power, James catch at the offer.  the party be still at

Beauchastel, and he devise go to his old quarter at Ebbscreek, and

make a descent upon they from thence.



when he come to take up his credential, he find James and his little

black leathern bag, determine to come at least to Ebbscreek with he,

and declare it make he frantic to stay at home and leave his cause

in other hand, and that he could not exist anywhere but close to the

scene of action.



Captain Hannaford be smoke in his demi-boat, and give his former

lodger a hearty welcome, but he twinkle knowingly with his eye, and

so significantly volunteer to inform they that the lady be still

at Beauchastel, that James's wrath at the old skipper's impudence begin

to revive, and he walk off to the remotest end of the garden.



the Captain, remain with Louis, with whom he be always on far more

easy term, look after the other gentleman, wink again, and

confess that he have suspect one or other of they might be come

that way this summer, though he could not say he have expect to see

they both together.



'Mind, Captain,' say Louis,' it ben't _i_ that make the boat late

this time last year.'



'well!  I might be wrong, I fancy you cast an eye that way.  then

maybe it ben't true what's all over the place here.'



Louis press to hear what.  'why, that when the French be go on

like Robert Spear and they old time, he have convoy the young lady

right through the midst of they, and they would both have be shoot, if

my Lady's butler haven't come down with a revolver, kill half-a-dozen

of the mob, and rescue they out of it, but that Lord Fitzjocelyn have

be desperately wound in go back to fetch her bracelet, and Mr.

Delaford have carry he out in his arm.'



'well!' say Louis, coolly, without alter a muscle of his face, as

the Captain look for an angry negative.



'and when they get home,--so the story went,--Mr. Frost, the tutor, be

so mad with jealousy and rage, that my Lady declare those mooring

would not suit she no long, but have let go, and lay her head right

for Beauchastel.'



'pray what be the young lady suppose to think of the matter?'



'story appear to vary.  one version say that Mr. Delaford have

find he on his knee to she; and that my Lady have snatch she

cruelly away, because she would not have she married before her own

daughter, and look over all the post, for fear there should be a

letter for she.  another declare that Miss Conway would not have he

at any price, and be set upon the poor tutor, and that he be lie

dangerously ill of a low fever. --The woman will have it so,' observe

the Captain, 'the story's everywhere, except maybe in the parlour at

Beauchastel, and I wouldn't wonder if Mrs. Mansell know it all herself,

for her maid have a tongue a yard long.  I willn't say but I think there

might be some grain of truth at the bottom--'



'and you shall hear it by-and-by, when I know what it be myself.'



'I'd not say I would have believe it the more if that fine gentleman

have take his oath of it--a fellow that ben't to be trust,' observe

the Captain.



this might have lead to a revelation, if Louis have have time to attend to

it; but he have pity on James's impatient misery, and proceed to ask

the loan of the boat.  the tide would not, however, serve; and as

wait till it would be not to be endure, the two cousin set off to

walk together through the wood, Louis beguile the way by chaff

James, as far as he would bear, with the idea of Isabel's name be

trifle with by the profane crowd.



he leave James at the gate of the park, prowl about like a panther to

try for a glimpse of Isabel's window, and feed his despair and

jealousy that Louis should boldly walk up to the door, while he, with

so much well a right, be exclude by his unguarded promise to Lady

Conway.



all the tumultuary emotion of his mind be endlessly repeat, and

many a slow and peal note of the church-clock have add fuel to his

impatience, and spur he to rush up to the door and claim his

right, before Louis came bound past the lodge-gate, flourish

his cap, and cry, 'Hurrah, Jem!  all right!'



'I'm go to she at once!' cry Jem, begin to rush off; but Louis

catch and imprison his arm.



'not so fast, sir!  you be to see she.  I promise you shall see she if

you wish it, but it must be in my aunt's way.'



'let I go, I say!'



'when I have walk five mile in your service, you willn't afford I an

arm to help I back.  I be not a horse with wing, and I willn't be

Cupid's post except on my own term.  come back.'



'I don't stir till I have hear the state of the case.'



'yes, you do; for all the sportsman will be come home, and my aunt

would not for all the world that Mr. Mansell catch you on the

forbid ground.'



'how can you give in to such shuffling nonsense!  if I be to claim

Isabel openly, why be I not to visit she openly?  you have yield to

that woman's crooked policy.  I don't trust you!'



'when you be her son, you may manage she as you please.  just now she

have we in her power, and can impose condition.  come on; and if you

be good, you shall hear.'



draw James along with he through the beechwood glade, he begin,

'you would have be more insane still if you have guess at my luck. I

find Isabel alone.  Mrs. Mansell have take the girl to some juvenile

fete, and Delaford be discreet enough not to rouse my aunt from her

letter.  I augur well from the happy conjunction.'



'go on; don't waste time in stuff.'



'Barkis be willing, then.  be that enough to the point?'



'Fitzjocelyn, you never have any feeling yourself, and therefore you

trifle with those of other.'



'I beg your pardon.  it be a shame!  Jem, you may be proud.  she

trust you completely, and whatever you think sufficient, she regard

as ample.'



'like she!  only too like she.  such confidence make one feel a

redouble responsibility.'



'I think I have find something at which you could not grumble.'



'how do she look?  how do they treat she?'



'apparently they have not yet feed she on bread and water.  no;

seriously, I must confess that she look uncommonly well and lovely!

never mind, Jem; I verily believe that, in spite of absence and all

that, she have never be so happy in her life.  if any description

could convey the sweetness of voice and manner when she speak of you! I

could not look in her face.  those look can only be for you.  we

talk it over, but she heed no way and mean; it be enough that

you be satisfied.  she say the subject have never be broach since

the flight from Northwold, and that Lady Conway's kindness never

vary; and she tell I she have little fear but that her dear mamma

would be prevail on to give sanction enough to hinder she from

feeling as if she be do wrong, or set a bad example to her

sister.  they know nothing of it; but Walter, who learn it no one

know how, draw the exemplary moral, that it serve his mother right

for inflict a tutor on he.'



'has she have my letter?  do she know I be here?'



'wait!  all this settle, and luncheon be ready, down come my Lady,

and we play unconsciousness to our good ability.  I must confess my

aunt beat we hollow!  Isabel then leave we to our conference, which we

conduct with the gravity of a tailor and an old woman make a match

in Brittany.'



'you come out with that valuable improvable freehold, the Terrace, I

suppose?'



'I tell the mere fact!  my aunt be rather grand about a

grammar-school; she say even a curacy would sound well, and she must

talk it over with Isabel.  I give your letter, conjure she to let

Isabel have it, and though she declare that it be no kindness, and

would put the poor darling into needless perplexity, she be touch

with my forbearance, in not having give it before, when I have such an

opportunity.  so she go away, and stay a weary while: but when she

come, it be worth the waiting.  she say Isabel be old enough to know

her own mind, and the attachment be so strong, and you so

unexceptionable, she do not think it possible to object: she have great

delight in see you make happy, and fulfil the dictate of her

own heart, now that it could be do with moderate prudence. they go to

Scarborough in a fortnight, and you will be welcome there. there's for

you!'



'Louis, you be the good fellow living!  but you say I be to see she

at once.'



'I ask, why wait for Scarborough?' and depict you hover

disconsolately round the precinct.  never mind, Jem, I do not make

you more ridiculous than human nature must need paint a lover, and it

be all to melt her heart.  I be start off to fetch you, when I

find she be in great terror.  she have never tell the Mansells of the

matter, and they must be prepare.  she cannot have it transpire while

she be in their house, and, in fact, be excessively afraid of Mr.

Mansell, and want to tell her story by letter.  now, I think,

consider all thing, she have a right to take her own way.'



'you say I be not to go without meet she!'



'I have assent, and be devise how to march off my lunatic quietly,

when the feminine goodnatured heart that be in she begin to relent, and

she look up in my face with a smile, and say the poor dear be

really exemplary, and if Isabel should walk to the beach and should

meet any one there, she need know nothing about it.'



'what say Isabel?'



'she hold up her stately head, and think it would be a well return

for Mr. Mansell's kindness to tell he herself before leave

Beauchastel; but Lady Conway entreat she not to be hasty, and

protest that her fear be of Mr. Mansell's displeasure with she for

not having take well care of she--she dread a break, and so

on,--till the end of it be, that though we agree that prudence would

carry we off to-morrow morning, yet her ladyship will look the other

way, if you happen to be on the southern beach at eleven o'clock

to-morrow morning.  I suppose you be very headlong and peremptory in

your note, for I could not imagine Isabel consent to a secret tryste

even so authorized.'



'I never ask for any such thing!  I would not for world see she lead

to do anything underhand.'



'she will honour you!  that's right, Jem!'



'neither as a clergyman, nor as a Dynevor, can I consent to trick even

those who have no claim to her duty!'



'neither as a gentleman, nor as a human creature,' add Louis, in the

same tone.  'shall I go back and give your answer?'



'no; you be walk lame enough already.'



'no matter for that.'



'to tell you the truth, I cann't stand your be with she again, while

I be make a fool of by that woman.  if I'm not to see she, I'll be off.

I'll send she a note; we will cross to Bickleypool, and start by the

mail-train this very night.'



Louis make no objection, and James hurry he into the little parlour,

where in ten minute the note be dash off:--





my own Most precious One!--(as, thank to my most unselfish of cousin,

I may dare to call you,)--I regret my fervency and urgency for an

interview, since it lead you to think I could purchase even such

happiness by a subterfuge unworthy of my calling, and an ill return of

the hospitality to which we owe our first meeting.  we will meet when

I claim you in the face of day, without the sense of steal felicity,

which be a charm to common-place mind.  my glory be in the assurance

that you understand my letter, approve, and be relieve.  with such

sanction, and with ardour before you like mine, I see that you could do

no other than consent, and there be not a shadow of censure in my mind;

but if, without compromise your sense of obedience, you could openly

avow our engagement to Mr. Mansell, I own that I should feel that we

be not draw into a compromise of sincerity.  what this cost I I

will not say; it will be bare existence till we meet at Scarborough.



                                       'your own, J. E. F. D.'





having write this and deposit it in the Ebbscreek post-office,

James bethink himself that his submissive cousin have throw himself

on the floor, with his bag for a pillow, try to make the most of the

few moment of rest before the midnight journey.  seize with

compunction, James exclaim, 'there, old fellow, we will stay

to-night.'



'thank you--' he be too sleepy for more.



the delay be recompense.  James be try to persuade Louis to rouse

himself to be revive by bread-and-cheese and beer, and could extort

nothing but a drowsy repetition of the rhyme, in old day the war-cry

of the Grammar-school against the present headmaster,--



     'the Welshman have like to be choke by a mouse,

      but he pull he out by the tail,'--



when an alarum come in the shape of a little grinning boy from

Beauchastel, with a note on which James have nearly lay hand, as he

see the writing, though the address be to the Viscount Fitzjocelyn.



'you may have it,' say Louis.  'if anything be want, the

coincidence prove that you be cut out for one another.  I rejoice

that the moon do not stoop from her sphere.'





'my Dear Cousin,--I trust to you to prevent Mr. F. Dynevor from be

hurt or disappoint; and, indeed, I scarcely think he will, though I

should not avail myself of the permission for meet he so kindly

intend.  I see at once that you feel as I do, and as I know he will.

he would not like I to have cause to blush before my kind friend--to

know that I have act a deceit, nor to set an example to my sister for

which they might not understand the justification.  I know that you

will obtain my pardon, if need; and to be assure of it, would be all

that would be require to complete the grateful happiness of



             'Isabel.'





the boy have order not to wait; and these be second by fear of

something that 'walk' in Ebbscreek wood after dark, he be go

before an answer could be think of.  it matter the less, since

Isabel must receive James's note early in the morning; and so, in fact,

she do--and she be blush over it, and feeling as if she could

never have bear to meet his eye but for the part she have fortunately

take, when Louisa tap at her door, with a message that Mr. Mansell

wish to speak with she, if she be ready.



she go down-stair still in a glow; and her old friend's first word

be a compliment on her rose, so point, that she doubt for a

moment whether he do not think they suspicious, especially as he put

his hand behind his back, and pace up and down the room, for some

moment.  he then come towards she, and say, in a very kind tone,

'Isabel, my dear, I send for you first, because I know your own mother

very well, my dear; and though Lady Conway be very kind, and have always

do you justice,--that I will always say for her,--yet there be time

when it may make a difference to a young woman whether she have her own

mother or not.'



Isabel's heart be beat.  she be certain that some discovery have

be make, and long to explain; but she be wise enough not to speak

in haste, and wait to see how the old gentleman would finally break

it to she.  he blunder on a little long, become more confused and

distress every minute, and at last come to the point abruptly.  'in

short, Isabel, my dear, what can you have do to set people say

that you have be correspond with the young man at ebbscreek?'



'I send a note to my cousin Fitzjocelyn last night,' say Isabel, with

such calmness, that the old gentleman fairly stand with his mouth open,

look at her aghast.



'Fitzjocelyn!  then it be Fitzjocelyn, be it?' he exclaim.  'then,

why could he not set about it openly and honourably?  do his father

object?  I would not have think it of you, Isabel, nor of the lad

neither!'



'you need not think it, dear Mr. Mansell.  there be nothing between

Lord Fitzjocelyn and myself but the warm friendship.'



'Isabel!  Isabel! why be you make mystery?  I do not wish to pry

into your affair.  I would have trust you anywhere; but when it

come round to I that you have be send a private messenger to one

of the young gentleman there, I don't know what to be at!  I would not

believe Mrs. Mansell at first; but I see the boy, and he say you have

send he yourself.  my dear, you may mean, very rightly--I be sure you

do, but you must not set people talk!  it be not act rightly by

I, Isabel; but I would not care for that, if it be act rightly by

yourself.'  and he gaze at she with a piteous, perplexed expression.



'let I call mamma,' say Isabel.



'as you will, my dear, but cannot you let the simple truth come out

between you and your own blood-relation, without all her word to come

between?  cann't you, Isabel?  I be sure you and I shall understand each

other.'



'that we shall,' reply Isabel, warmly.  'I have give she no promise.

dear Mr. Mansell, I have wish all along that you should know that I

be engage, with her full consent, to Mr. Frost Dynevor.'



'to the little black tutor!' cry Mr. Mansell, recoiling, but

recollect himself.  'I beg your pardon, my dear, he may be a very

good man, but what become of all this scramble over barricade with

the young Lord?'



Isabel describe the true history of her engagement; and it be

receive with a long, low whistle, by no mean too complimentary.



'and what make he come and hide in hole and corner, if this be all

with your mamma's good will?'



'Mamma think you would be displease; she insist on take her own

time for break it to you,' say Isabel.



'be there ever a woman but must have her mystery?  well, I should have

like he well if he have not give into it!'



'he never do!' say Isabel, indignant enough to disclose in full the

whole arrangement make by Lady Conway's manoeuvre and lax good-nature.

'I know it would never do,' she add, 'though I could not say so

before she and Fitzjocelyn.  my note be to tell they so: and look

here, Mr. Mansell, this be what Mr. Dynevor have already write before

receive mine.'



she hold it out proudly; and Mr. Mansell, make an unwilling sound

between his tooth, take it from she; but, as he read, his countenance

change, and he exclaim, 'ha! very well!  this be something like! so

that's it, be it?  you and he would not combine to cheat the old man,

like a pair of lover in a trumpery novel!'



'no, indeed!' say Isabel, 'that would be a bad way of beginning.'



'where be the young fellow?--at ebbscreek, do you say?  I'll tell you

what, Isabel,' with his hand on the bell, 'I'll have out the dogcart

this minute, and fetch he home to breakfast, to meet my Lady when she

come down stair, if it be only for the sake of show that I like

plain dealing!'



'isabel could only blush, smile, look doubtful, and yet so very happy

and grateful, that Mr. Mansell become cautious, lest his impulse should

have carry he too far, and, after having order the vehicle to be

prepare, he catch she by the hand, and detain she, say, 'mind

you, Miss, you be not to take this for over-much.  I'm afraid it be a

silly business, and I do not want you to throw yourself away on a

schoolmaster.  I must see and talk to the man myself; but I willn't have

anything that's not open and above-board, and that my Lady shall see

for once in her life!'



'I'm not afraid,' say Isabel, smile.  'James will make his own way

with you.'



Isabel run away to excuse and explain her confession to Lady Conway;

while Mr. Mansell indulge in another whistle, and then go to inform

his wife that he be afraid the girl have be make a fool of herself;

but it be not Lady Conway's fault that she be nothing bad, and he

be resolve, whatever he do, to show that honesty be the only thing

that would go down with he.



the boat be rock on the green wave, and Louis be in the act of

wave an adieu to deaf Mrs. Hannaford, when a huntsman's halloo cause

James to look round and behold Mr. Mansell stand up in his dogcart,

make energetic signal with his whip.



he have mean to be very guard, and wait to judge of James before

show that he approve, but the excitement of the chase betray he

into a glow of cordiality, and he shake hand with vehemence.



'that's right!--just in time!  jump in, and come home to breakfast. so

you wouldn't be a party to my Lady's tricks!--just like she--just as

she wheedle poor Conway.  I will let she see how I esteem plain

deal!  I don't say that I see my way through this business; but

we'll talk it over together, and settle matter without my Lady.'



James hardly know where he be, between joy and surprise.  the

invitation be extend to his companion; but Fitzjocelyn discern

that both James and Mr. Mansell would prefer be leave to themselves;

he have a repugnance to an immediate discussion with the one aunt, and

be in haste to carry the tiding to the other: and besides, it be

become possible that letter might arrive from the traveller.

actuate by all these motive, he decline the offer of hospitality,

and row across to Bickleypool, enlighten the Captain on the state

of affair as far as he desire.









CHAPTER II.



the THIRD TIME.



  Tho' this be fair, and that be braw,

    and you the toast of all the town,

  I sigh and say, amang they a',

    Ye be not Mary Morison.

                            BURNS.





Mrs. Frost and Louis be very merry over the result of Lady Conway's

stratagem, and sit up indulge in bright anticipation until so late

an hour, that Louis be compel to relinquish his purpose of go

home that night, but he persist in walk to Ormersfield before

breakfast, that he might satisfy himself whether there be any letter.



it be a brisk October morning, the sportsman's gun and whistle

re-echo from the hill side; where here and there appear the dog

career along over green turnip-field or across amber stubble. the

Little Northwold tree, in dark, sober tint of brown and purple, hung

over the grey wall, tint by hoary lichen; and as Louis enter the

Ormersfield field path, and plunge into his own Ferny dell, the long

grass and bracken hang over the path, weigh down with silvery dew,

and the large cavernous web of the autumnal spider be all one thick

flake of wet.



if he could not enter the ravine without thankfulness for his past

escape, neither could he forget gratitude to she who have come to his

relief from hopeless agony!  he quicken his pace, in the earnest

longing for tiding, which have seize he, even to heart sickness.



it be the reaction of the ardour and excitement that have so long

possess he.  the victory have be gain--he have be oblige to

leave James to work in his own cause, and would be no long want in

the same manner by his cousin.  the sense of loneliness, and of the

want of an object, come strongly upon he as he walk through the prim

old solitary garden, and look up at the dreary window of the house,

almost reluctant to enter, as long as it be without Mary's own serene

atmosphere of sympathy and good sense, her precious office of love,

her clear steady eye, even in babyhood his trustworthy counsellor.



be it a delusion of fancy, act on reflection in the glass, that,

as he mount the step from the lawn, depict Mary's figure through

the dining-room window?  Nay, the table be really lay for

breakfast--a female figure be actually stand over the tea-chest.



'a scene from the Vicar of Wakefield delude I,' decide Louis,

advance to the third window, which be open.



it be Mary Ponsonby.



'Mary!'



'you here?--They say you be not at home!'



'my father!--Where?'



'he be not come down.  he be as well as possible.  we come at eleven

last night.  I find I be not want,' add Mary, with a degree of

agitation, that make he conclude that she have lose her father.



one step he make to find the Earl, but too much excited to move away or

to stand still, he come towards she, wring her hand in a more real way

than in his first bewildered surprise, and exclaim in transport, 'o

Mary! Mary! to have you back again!' then, remember his inference,

add, low and gravely, 'it make I selfish--I be not think of

your grief.'



'never mind,' say Mary, smile, though her eye overflow, 'I must

be glad to be at home again, and such a welcome as this--'



'o Mary, Mary!' he cry, nearly beside himself, 'I have not know what

to do without you!  you will believe it now, willn't you?'--oh, willn't

you?'



Mary would have be a wonderful person have she not instantly and

utterly forget all her conclusion from Frampton's having declare

he go to Beauchastel for an unlimited time; but all she do be to

turn away her crimson tearful face, and reply, 'your father would not

wish it now.'



'then the speculation have fail?  so much the well!'



'no, no! he must tell you--'



she be try to withdraw her hand, when Lord Ormersfield open the

door, and in the moment of his amazed 'Louis!' Mary have flee.



'what be it? oh! what be it, father? cry Louis for all greeting, 'why

can she say you would not wish it now?'



'wish it? wish what?' ask the Earl, without the intuitive perception

of the meaning of the pronoun.



'what you have always wish--Mary and I--what be the only happiness

that life can offer I!'



'if I wish it a year ago, I could only wish it the more now,' say

the Earl.  'but how be this?--I fully believe you commit to Miss

Conway.'



'Miss Conway!  Miss Conway!' burst out Louis, in a frenzy.  'because

what a cloud on the happy morning!

CHAPTER III.



mist.



  therefore thine eye through mist of many day

    shines bright; and beauty, like a linger rose,

  sit on thy cheek, and in thy laughter play;

    while wintry frost have fall on thy foe,

  and, like a vale that breathe the western sky,

  Thy heart be green, though summer be go by.

                                      F. TENNYSON.





Happy Aunt Kitty!--the centre, the confidante of so much love! perhaps

her enjoyment be the most keen and pure of all, because the most free

from self--the most devoid of those care for the morrow, which, after

beset middle life, often so desert old age as to render it as free

and fresh as childhood.  she have know the bad: she have be bear

through by heart-whole faith and love, she have see how often fretting

for the future be vain, and experience that anticipation be bad

than reality.  where there be true affection and sound trust, she

could not, would not, and do not fear for those she love.



James go backwards and forwards in stormy happiness.  he have come to

a comfortable understanding with old Mr. Mansell, who have treat he

Frost Dynevor be graciously pleased to accept he as a worthy relation.

the marriage be fix for the end of November, so as to give time for

Poor Louis be often mortify into doubt of his own ability to

the curtain ring, while Aunt Catharine go to inspect the workman,

and many a time be her care forget, and her active spirit

resume, while Louis act carpenter under her direction, and

rectify error of the workman.  it might not be poetical, but the

french sky-blue paper, cover with silvery fern-leave, that Louis

take such pain to procure, and the china door-handle that he bring

over in his pocket, and the great map which Mary paste over the

obstinate spot of damp in the vestibule, be the occasion of the

great blitheness and merriment that they share together.  much do

they enjoy the prediction that James would not know his own house;

greatly do they delight in sow surprise, and in obtain Aunt

Catharine's never-fail start of well-pleased astonishment.  each

wedding present be an event;--Mr. Mansell's piano, which disconcert

all previous design; Lord Ormersfield's handsome plate; and many a

minor gift from old scholar, delight to find an occasion when an

offering would not be an offence.  even Mr. Calcott give a valuable

inkstand, in which Mrs. Frost and Louis beheld something of forgiveness.



Isabel have express a wish that Mary should be one of her bridesmaid.

a wedding be not the scene which poor Mary wish to witness at

present; but she see Louis bent on have she with he, and would not

vex he by reluctance.  he have also prevail on his father to be

present, though the Earl be much afraid of establish a precedent,

and be ask to act the part of father on future contingency.

there be only one bride, as he tell Louis, whom he could ever wish to

give away.  however, that trouble be spare he by Mr. Mansell; but

still Louis would not let he off, on the plea that James's side of the

house should make as impose a demonstration as possible.



Mrs. Frost be less manageable.  though warmly invite by the Conways,

and fondly entreat by her grandson, she shake her head, and say she

be past those thing, and that the old mother always stay at home to

cook the wedding dinner.  she should hear all when Clara come home the

next day, and should be ready for the happy pair when they would return

for Christmas, after a brief stay at Thornton Conway, which Isabel

wish James to see, that he might share in all her old association.



all the rest of the party journey to London on a November day; and,

in gaslight and gloom, they deposit Mary at her aunt's house in

Bryanston Square.



Gaslight be the staple of Hymen's torch the next morning.  London be

under one of the fog, of which it be popularly say you may cut they

with a knife.  the church be in dim twilight; the bride and bridegroom

loom through the haze, and the indistinctness make Clara's fine tall

figure appear quite majestic above the head of the other bridesmaid.



the breakfast be by lamp-light, and the mist look lurid and grim

over the white cake, and no one talk of anything but the comparative

density of fog; and Mr. Mansell's asthma have come on, and his speech

be devolve upon Lord Ormersfield, to whom Louis have imprudently

promise exemption.



what be bad, Lady Conway have pair they off in the order of

precedence; and Louis be a victim to two dowager, between whom he

could neither see nor speak to Mary.  he be the more concerned,

because he have think she look depressed and avoid his eye.



he try to believe this caution, but he think she be also elude

party be to dine with Lady Conway; and, trust in the meantime to

discover what be on her spirit, he try to resign himself to the

when the married pair take leave, Walter give his sister a great hug,

but have no perception of his office of hand she downstair; and it

leave the fog in London.



she smile dreamily, and repeat, 'beautiful,' as though all be so

beautiful already to she that she do not so much as perceive the fog.

happy next.'



'you do not look so,' say Clara, earnestly.



the two sister have come partly downstairs, but their London habit have

and now they have rush up again, while Clara, with one foot on the

staircase, look in her cousin's face, as he try to smile in answer,

and repeat, 'Louis, I hope you be quite happy.'



'I be,' say Louis, quickly.



'then why do you look so grave and uneasy?'



'Louis!' say an entreating voice above, and there stand Mary--'Pray

say nothing, but call a cab for I, please.  no, I be not ill--indeed,

I be not--but I cannot stay!'



'you look ill!  it have be too much for you!  Clara, take she--let she

lie down quietly,' cry Louis, spring to her side.



'oh no, thank you-no,' say Mary, decidedly, though very low; 'I tell

Lady Conway that I could not stay.  I settle it with Aunt Melicent.'



'that aunt of yours--'



'hush!  no, it be for my own sake--my own doing.  I cannot bear it any

long!  please let I go!'



'then I will take you.  I see the brougham wait.  we will go quietly

together.'



'no, that must not be.'

much.  my poor Mary!  that be what come of do what I like instead

come; nay, if you will not, at least let Clara go with you, and come

Mary rouse herself at last to speak, as she move downstairs--'You

need not think of I; there be nothing the matter with I.  I promise

Aunt Melicent to come home.  she be very kind--it be not that.'

be with you the first thing tomorrow.'



'yes, you must come to-morrow,' say Mary, in a tone he could not

he stand hesitate for a moment as it drive off; then, instead of

enter the house, walk off quickly in the same direction.



Clara have stand all the time like a statue on the stair, wait to

see if she be want, and gaze intently, with her finger clasp.

when both be go she draw a long breath, and nod with her head,

whisper to herself, in a grave and critical voice--'That be love!'

down--I believe they never tell she I be there.'



'it be all that aunt--horrid woman!'



'don't talk of it now.  I _will_ see she to-morrow.'



Clara grieve for he whenever she see he call on to exert himself

to talk; and she even guard he from the sally of his young

cousin.  once, when much music and talk be go on, he come and sit

part with her schoolfellow; and how some of her old foe have become,

as she hope, friend for life; but she see his eye fix and absent

like to hear;' and with a manifest effort he bend his mind to attend.



'oh!' think Clara, as she go up that night--'why will the day one

most expect to be happy turn out so much otherwise?  however, he will

manage to tell I all about it when he and his father take I home

to-morrow.'









CHAPTER IV.



OUTWARD BOUND.



  the voice which I do more esteem

    than music in her sweetest key--

  those eye which unto I do seem

    more comfortable than the day--

  those now by I, as they have be,

  shall never more be hear or see.

                              GEORGE WITHER.





in suspense and impatience, Fitzjocelyn await the end of his father's

breakfast, that he might hasten to learn what ail Mary. the post come

in, vex he at first merely as an additional delay, but presently a

sound of dissatisfaction attract his notice to the foreign air of two

envelope which have be forward from home.



'Hem!' say the Earl, gravely, 'I be afraid this fellow Ponsonby will

give we some trouble.'



'then Mary have hear from he!' cry Louis.  'she be keep it from

I, not to spoil the day.  I must go to she this moment--'but pausing

again, 'what be it?  he cannot have have my letter!'



'no, but he seem to have anticipate it.  puff up as they be about

these speculation, he imagine I to have bring Mary home for no

purpose but to repair our fortune; and inform I that, in the event

of your marriage, she will receive not a farthing beyond her mother's

settlement.  I be much oblige!  it be all I ever think you would

receive; and but for I, it would have be in the bottom of some mine

long ago!  do you wish to see what he say?'



Louis catch up the missive.  it be the letter of a very angry man,

too violent to retain the cold formality which he try to assume. 'he

be beholden to his lordship for his solicitude about his daughter.  it

be of a piece with other assistance formerly render to he in his

domestic arrangement, for which he be equally oblige.  he be happy

to inform his lordship that, in this instance, his precaution have be

uncalled for; and refer he to a letter which he would receive from

Mr. Dynevor by the same mail, for an explanation of the circumstance

to which he refer.  he have be inform, by undoubted authority,

that Lord Fitzjocelyn have do his daughter the honour of solicit

her hand.  it might console his lordship to learn that, should the

union take place, the whole of his property would be secure to Mrs.

Ponsonby, and his daughter's sole fortune would be that which she

inherit by her mother's marriage settlement.  possibly this

intelligence might lead to a cessation of these flattering attention.'



'Mrs. Ponsonby! he can mention she in the same sentence with Mary's

mother!' say the Earl.



Louis turn pale as he read, and scarcely breathe as he look up at

for.  it be not the first letter I have have from he. I find he have

some just cause for offence.  the marriage be less disgraceful than I

have be lead to believe.  here be Oliver Dynevor's testimony.'



Oliver Dynevor's be a succinct business-like letter, certify his

Rosita de Guzman be an orphan of a very respectable family, who have

come to spend the year before her intend noviciate at the house of an

have be guilty of no impropriety; she be visit by every one; and

that there be no reason against Mary Ponsonby associate with she.



'what could the clerk be think of?' exclaim Louis.



'my first impression be not take from the clerk.  what I hear first,

and in the strong term, be from the captain of a ship at

'I be come with you,' say the Earl.  'she will require my personal

on arrive, they be usher into the room where Miss Ponsonby be at

at that moment, when Louis's face be crimson with confusion and

'I could not spoil their wedding-day, that we have wish for so long.'

'in the morning.  oh, that I have examine far!  oh, that I have

he cry out with deprecate horror.

the admission could only do Louis's heart good, and he recur to his

'you do not know all,' say Mary.  'I could not show you his letter;

but, from it and from my aunt, I well understand what impression he

'tell I!'



she could not help give herself the relief, when that most loving,

to hinder his pleading against her self-conviction, she re-open she

CHAPTER V.



the new world.



  still onward, as to southern sky,

  we spread our sail, new star arise,

  new light upon the glance tide,

  fresh hue where pearl and coral hide:

  what be they all but token true

  of grace for ever fresh and new!

                        Prayers for Emigrants.





there be some day in the early year, devoid indeed of spring

brilliance, but full of soft, heavy, steaming fragrance, pervade the

grey air with sweet odour, and foster the growth of tender bud and

fragile stem with an unseen influence, more mild and kindly than even

the smile sunbeam or the gush shower.  'a grow day,' as the

country-people term such genial, gentle weather, might not be without

analogy to the brief betrothal of Louis and Mary.



subdue and anxious, there have be little of the ordinary light of

joy, hope, or gaiety, and their pleasure have be less their own than

in prepare the happiness of their two friend.  it be a time such as

to be more sweet in memory than it be in the present; and the shade

which have hang over it, the self-restraint and the forbearance which it

have elicit, have unconsciously conduce to the development of the

character of both, prepare they to endure the parting far more

effectually than unmixed enjoyment could have do. the check upon

Louis's love of trifling, the restraint on his spirit, the being

throw back on his own judgment when he want to lean upon Mary, have

give he a habit of control his boyish way.



it be a call to train himself in manliness and self-reliance.  it

change he from the unstable reed he once have be, and help he to

go, he care little what become of he until her letter could

arrive; and his father, with more attention to his suppose benefit

than to his wish, carry he at once, without return home, to a

round of visit among all his acquaintance most likely to furnish a

distracting amount of Christmas gaiety.  in the midst of these, there

occur a vacancy in the representation of a borough chiefly under the

influence of Sir Miles Oakstead; and, as it be consider expedient

that he should be bring into Parliament, his father repair with he

at once to Oakstead, and involve he in all the business of the

election.  on his success, he go with his father to London for the

session, and this be all that his friend at Northwold know of he.

he write hurried note to James or to Mr. Holdsworth on necessary

instead of feeling, and promise to write to Aunt Catharine when he

should have time; but the time do not seem to come, and it be easy to

believe that his passiveness of will, increase by the recent stroke,

have cause he to be hurry into a condition of involuntary practical

activity.



Mary, meanwhile, be retrace her voyage, in the lull of spirit

which, after long straining, have nothing to do but to wait in patience,

brace themselves for a fresh trial.  never suffer herself, at sea,

her first feeling, after the final wrench of parting, be interrupt

yet Mary be not so much absorb and satisfied with the visible and

she be sensible of the change again and again, when sight recur

which once have only speak to her eye.  that luminous sea, sparkle

like flood of star, have be little more than 'how pretty! how

funny!' at her first voyage.  now, it be not only 'how Louis would

the globe with mysterious beauty! how marvellously have he cause his

of that path 'like a shine light, shine more and more unto the

perfect day.'  if with it come the remembrance of his vision of the

thread of light, it be not a recollection which would lead to

repine.



and when at Cape Horn, a mighty ice mountain drift within view,

spire, pinnacle, encrust with whiteness, rival only by the glory

of the summer cloud, cavern here and there into hollow of sapphire

blue, too deeply dazzle to behold, or rise into peak of clear,

hard, chill green; the wild fantastic point sometimes glimmer with

fragment of the rainbow arch; the rich variety, endless beyond measure

in form and colouring, and not only magnificent and terrible in the

whole mass, but lovely beyond imagination in each crystal too minute

for the eye.  Mary have once, on a like occasion, only say, 'it be

very cold;' and look to see whether the captain expect the monster

the sublime aspiration which gothic cathedral seem as if they would

fain embody.  and then, she think of the marvellous interminable

waste of beauty of those untrodden region, whence yonder enormous

iceberg be but a small fragment--a petty messenger--region unseen by

human eye--beauty untouched by human hand-the glory, the sameness, yet

the infinite variety of perfect purity.  do it not seem, with all the

association of cold, of peril, of dreariness, to be a visible token

that indeed he who fashion it could prepare 'good thing past man's

understanding!'



it be well for Mary that southern constellation, snowy, white-wing

then she be full of hope and eager ardour to arrive, long for the

'no.'



'she have sail about three week.  she take home your cousin, Mr.

that no one have meet she--Valdivia not expect so soon--not anticipate

'my father desire that I would come out by she,' say Mary.



'ay, true--so he inform I; but since later intelligence'--and he

cast a glance at Mary, to judge how much furth to go; but meeting

with nothing but severity, he cover the impertinence by say, 'in

fact, though the Valdivia be mention, and Mrs. Willis, Mr. Ponsonby

have reason to suppose you would not receive his letter in time to

avail yourself of the escort.'



'I do so, however,' say Mary, coldly.



'most gratifying.  Mr. and Mrs. Ponsonby will be highly gratified. in

fact, Miss Ponsonby, I must confess that be a most unfortunate blunder

be so long absent at Guayaquil that I have have no opportunity of

your feeling, unaware as I be then of the amiable quality of the

Robson; it be very unfortunate, but it cannot be help. pray can you

tell I where the lad Madison be?' she add, resolve to show he that

'he be at the San Benito mine, Miss Ponsonby.'



'how do he go on?'



'well--I may say very well, allow for inexperience.  he appear a

steady, intelligent lad, and I have no doubt will answer the purpose

well.'



there be one gratification for Mary, at least, in the pleasure this

would afford at home; but Robson continue make conversation about

ten minute more, half spend in conquer her disgust, half in sick

then, instantly change his language to Spanish, he add, appeal

to his wife, that have they guess she be on board, they would have

come to meet she.



Rosita reply earnestly to that effect, and warmly embrace Mary,

pity she for such an arrival, and hope that dolore have make she

and simplicity that be very win; and her husband glance more

but he carefully avert his glance, and she could read nothing.



Weary as she be, Mary hear again and again, through her unglazed

she need not have spend so many hour in preparation for the morning.

she be up early, in hope of see her father before he go to his

the midday meal bring her father--still kind and affectionate, but

and fear could not even let she dream rest.



then come a drive with Rosita, resplendent in french millinery, then

might have enjoy but for her suspense.



on the third, Rosita be make happy by unpack an elegant little

be ever two sister-in-law more unlike?   but Lord Ormersfield have

whether Rosita would have comprehend this without Mary's blush

father forbid when your father refuse your portion?'



'oh no, he would receive I if I bring nothing.'



'and you wish to marry?' say Rosita.



'if my father would only consent.'



'but why do you come here then?' say Rosita, open her large eye.



'my father command I.'



'England be a long way off,' say Rosita, languidly, 'he could not have

reach you there.  you would have be a great lady and noble! how

could you come away, if he would still have you?'



'because it would have be wrong.  we could not have be happy in

disobey my father.'



'ah! but you could have do penance.  I have many penance to do for

quit my convent; Padre Inigo be very severe, but they be over at

forgive I, and send I so many silver flower and dulce; I will show

they to you some day.  could you not have do penance?'



'I be afraid not.'



'ah! I forget you be a heretic, poor thing!  how inconvenient!  and

so you will not come with I to the bull-fight next Sunday?'



such be Rosita's idea on the point, Mary give up much hope in her

influence, and try what a good-humour announcement of she

re-establishment of the english breakfast would effect towards bring

'so you shall, my dear, but we be all tired; we must have our night's

rest.'



'no, papa, it must be to-night, if you please.  it be necessary for I

to know before to-morrow how I be to write to Lord Fitzjocelyn.'



'Pshaw!  Mary, I've settle that young fellow!'



'Papa, I don't think you know--'



'I've write he a civil answer, if that's what you mean, much

civiller than he or his father deserve,' he say, speak loud, and

'I know this, that old Ormersfield have be the curse of my life!' and

'there be very few people in this world half so good or so

do you! but it be not a heartbreaking matter either, my girl!'



this be too much for Mary, and when her father would have kiss she,

she lay her head on his shoulder and weep silently but bitterly.



'ha! what's all this?  why, you don't pretend to care for a young

mercenary scamp like that?'



'he be the noble, most generous, most disinterested man I ever know!'

say Mary, stand apart, and speak clearly.  'I give he up

'ha! ha! so long as you give he up, we willn't quarrel.  he shall be all

'I do not mean to mention he again,' say Mary; 'I wish to obey you.'



'then there's an end of the matter.  you'll get over it, my girl, and

to-morrow in the sala, like old time.  Goodnight, my dear.'



wait upon himself!  that be the reward that Mr. Ponsonby hold out

to his daughter for crush her first love!



but it be a reward.  anything that draw her father nearer to she be

receive with gratitude by Mary, and the word of kindness in some

degree soften the blow. she have never have much hope, though now she

find it have be more than she have be willing to believe; and even

not any one who could love he so well--oh no!  but when Mary find she

thought take this turn, she rise up as she lay, clasp her hand

together, and repeat half aloud again and again, 'be Thou my all!'



and by the morning, though Mary's cheek be very white, and her eye

sink for want of sleep, she have a cheerful word for her father, and a

smile, the very sight of which would have go to the heart of any one

of those from whom he have cut she off.



then she write her letter.  it be not so hard to make this final

severance as it have be to watch Louis's face, and think of the pain

she have to inflict.  many a time have she weigh each phrase she set

down, so that it might offend neither against sincerity nor

resignation, and yet be soothe and console.  some would have

think her letter stiff and laboured, but she have learn to believe

that a grave and careful style befit a serious occasion, and would

have think incoherency childish or affected.



she release he entirely from his engagement, entreat he not to

rebel against the decision, but to join she in thankfulness that no

shade need be cast over the remembrance of the happy hour spend

together; and beg he not to grieve, since she have, after the first

pain, be able to acquiesce in the belief that the separation might

conduce to his happiness; and she should always regard he as one of

those most near and dear to she, and rejoice in whatever be for his

welfare, glad that his heart be still young enough to form new tie.

'forgive I for speak thus,' she add; 'I know that it may wound

you now, but there may come a time when it may make you feel more at

ease and unfettered; and I could not endure to imagine that the

affection which you bring yourself to lavish on one so unworthy,

should stand in the way of your happiness for life.'  she desire he

to make no answer, but to consider this as the final dissolution: and

she conclude by all that she think would prove most console, as to

the present state of affair with she; and with a few affectionate

word, to show that he be still a great deal to she, though everything

he might not be.



this do, Mary face her life in the New World.  she have to form she

habit for herself, for her importance in the house be go; but she

go to work resolutely, and, lonely as she be, she have far more

resource than if she have never be at Ormersfield.  she have many

hour to herself, and she unpack her book, and set herself course

of study, to which Louis have open the door.  she unveil her eye to

natural history, and do not find flower or butterfly unsoothe. she

undertake the not very hopeful task of teach a tiny negro imp, who

answer the purpose of a bell, to read and work; and she be

persevere in her effort to get xavi and dolore to make her father

comfortable.



her father be decidedly glad of her company.  he like conversation,

and enjoy the morning meeting, to which Mr. Ward be often a welcome

addition, delight in anything so English, and find Miss Ponsonby

much improve by her introduction to english society. sometimes Mary

write for her father, and now and then be consult; and she be

always grateful for whatever make she feel herself of use.  she be on

kind and friendly term with Rosita, but they do not become more

intimate than at first.  the Senora be swinge in a hammock

half-asleep, with a cigarette between her lip, all the morning; and

when she emerge from this torpid state, in a splendid toilette, she

have too many more congenial friend often to need her step-daughter in

her visit, her expedition to lottery, and her call on her old

friend the nun.  on a fast-day, or any other occasion that keep she

at home, she either arrange her jewel, discuss her dress, or have

some lively chatter, which she call learn English.  she coax,

fondle, and domineer prettily over Mr. Ponsonby; and he look on

amused, gratify her caprice, caress she, and seem to regard she

as a pretty pet and playthe.









CHAPTER VI.



the two PENDRAGONS.



  the red dragon and the white,

  hard together gin they smite,

  with mouth, paw, and tail,

  between hem be full hard batail.

                 the history of Merlin.





spring be on the border of summer, when one afternoon, as Clara sat

write a note in the drawing-room, she hear a tap at the door of the

little sitting-room, and spring to open it, she behold a welcome

sight.



'Louis!  how glad I be!  where do you come from?'



'last from the station,' say Louis.



'what make you knock at that door, now the drawing-room be alive?'



'I could not venture on an unceremonious invasion of Mrs. James Frost's

territory.'



'you'll find no distinction of territory here,' laugh Clara.  'it be

a fiction that we be to live in separate room, like naughty

child.  do not the drawing-room look nice?'



'as much improve as the inhabitant.  where be the other native?'



'Granny and Isabel be walk, and will end by pick up Jem come

out of school.  we use to wait for he so often, that at last he say

we should be laugh at, so there's a law against it which no one dare

to transgress but granny.'



'so I conclude that you be a happy family.'



'after all, it be worth spend two year at school to enjoy properly

the have it over.'



'I give Jem credit for having secure a first-rate governess for you.'



'that she be!  why, with she I really do like read and draw all

the morning!  I almost believe that some day I shall wake up and find

myself an accomplished young lady!  and, Louis, have you read the last

Western Magazine?'



'I have read very little for sport lately.'



'then I must tell you.  Jem be bemoan himself about have nothing

to give to the new Blind Asylum, and the next evening Isabel bring

out the prettiest little manuscript book, tie with blue ribbon, and

tell he to do as he please with it.  it be a charming account of her

expedition to the Hebrides, write out for her sister, without a

notion of anything far; but Jem send it to this Magazine, and it be

accept, and the first part be out.  she will have quite a sum for it,

and all be to go to the Blind Asylum!'



'Capital!--Let I take it home to night, Clara, and I will stand an

examination on it to-morrow.'



'we ask she whether she project a sketch of the Paris Revolution,'

say Clara, laugh.  'she have a famous heap of manuscript in her

desk, and one long story about a Sir Roland, who have his name before

she know Jem, but it be all unfinished, she tear out a great many

page, and have to make a new finish; and I be afraid the poor knight be

go to die of a mortal wound at his lady's foot.  Isabel like sad

thing best;--but oh! here they come, and I'm talk dreadful treason.'



three more joyous-look people could hardly have be find than

those who enter the room, welcome Louis with delight, and ask

what good wind have bring he.



'partly that Inglewood be cry out for the master's eye,' say Louis;

'and partly that my father fancy I look fag, and kindly let I

run down for a holiday.'



'I be of his mind,' say Mrs. Frost, tenderly; 'there be an M.P.

expression gathering on your brow, Louis.'



'for you to dispel, Aunt Kitty.  I tell he you be the good

dissipation, and Virginia be of the same mind.  Isabel, she say

Dynevor Terrace be the only place she ever wish to see again.'



'do you often see Virginia?' ask Isabel.



'not unless I go early, and beg for she; and then she generally have

some master.  that last onset of accomplishment be serious!'



'yes,' say Isabel, 'the sense of leisure and tranquillity here be

marvellous!'



'not leisure in the sense of idleness,' say James.



'no,' say Isabel; 'but formerly idle requirement throng my time,

and for nothing worth do could I find leisure.'



'there be nothing more exact than idle requirement,' say James.

'pray be Clara accept that invitation?  come to dinner, Louis, and

give we an excuse.'



'no, he willn't,' say Mrs. Frost, 'he will take my side.  these young

people want to cast off all their neighbour.'



'now, granny,' exclaim James, 'have we not dutifully dine all round?

do not isabel conduct Clara to that ball?  be it not hard to reproach

we with sigh at an evening immolate at the shrine of the

richardson?'



'well, my dear, you must judge.'



'I be ready to do whatever you think right; I leave you to settle it,'

say Isabel, move out of the room, that Louis might be free for a

more intimate conversation.



'now,' cry James, 'be it in the nature of thing that she should live

in such society as Mrs. Walby's and Mrs. Richardson's?  People who call

her Mrs. James!'



'such a queen as she look among they!' say Clara.



'one comfort be, they don't like that,' say James.  'even Mrs. Calcott

be not flatter by her precedence.  I hope we shall soon be drop

out of their party.  as long as I do my duty by their son, what

right have they to impose the penance of their society on my wife?  all

the irksomeness of what she have leave, and none of the compensation!'



'blissful solitude' say Louis, 'thereto I leave you.'



'you be not go yet!  you mean to dine here?' be the cry.



'my dear friend,' he say, hold up his hand, 'if you only know how

I long to have no one to speak to!'



'you cry out for silence!' exclaim James.



'I be pant for what I have not have these five month--space for my

thought to turn round.'



'surely you be at liberty to form your own habit!' say James.



'I be tell so whenever my father see I receive a note,' say Louis,

wearily; 'but I see that, habituate as he be to live alone, he be

never really at ease unless I be in the way; so I make our hour agree

as far as our respective treadmill permit; and though we do not speak

much, I can never think in company.'



'don't you have your ride to yourself?'



'why, no.  my father will never ride enough to do he good, unless he

want to do I good.  People be all surprised to see he look so

well; the country lane make he quite bloom.'



'but not you, my poor boy,' say his aunt; 'I be afraid it be a sad

strain.'



'there now, Aunt Kitty, I be go.  I must have the pleasure of look

natural sometimes, without cause any vituperation of any one beyond

sea.'



'you shall look just as you please if you will only stay.  we be just

go to dinner.'



'thank you, let I come to-morrow.  I shall be well company when I

have have my sulk out.'



his aunt follow he to the stair, and he turn to she, say,

anxiously, 'no letter?'  she shake her head.  'it would be barely

possible,' he say, 'but if it would only come while I be at home in

peace!'



'ah! this be sadly try!' say she, part his hair on his brow as

he stand some step below she, and win a sweet smile from he.



'all for the good,' he say.  'one thing may mitigate another.  that

political whirlpool might suck I in, if I have any heart or hope for

it.  and, on the other hand, it would be very unwholesome to be leave to

my own inertness--to be as good for nothing as I feel.'



'my poor dear boy, you be very good about it.  I wish you could have

be spare.'



'I do not come to make you sad, Aunt Kitty,' he reply, smile; 'no;

I get some energy back when I remember that this may be a probation.

her mother would not have think I man enough, and that be what I

have to work for.  whether this end well or not, she be the lead

star of my life.'  and, with the renewal of spirit with which he have

speak, he press his aunt's hand, and run down stair.



when he ride to Northwold, the follow afternoon, having spend the

morning in walk over his field, he overtake a most comfortable

couple--James and Isabel, return from their holiday stroll, and

Louis, leave his horse at the inn, and join they, begin to hear

all their school affair.  James have throw his whole heart into his

work, have be make various reform, introduce new study, make

a point of religious instruction, and meditate on a course of

lecture on history, to be give in the evening, the attendance to be

voluntary, but a prize hold out for proficiency.  Louis take up the

subject eagerly, and Isabel enter into the discussion with all her

soul, and the grammar-school do indeed seem to be in a way to become

something very superior in tone to anything Northwold have formerly

see, engross as it do all the power of a man of such ability, in

the full vigour of youth.



talk earnestly, the trio have reach the Terrace, and James be

unlatche the iron gate, when he interrupt himself in the midst of

detail his view on modern language to say, 'no, I have nothing for

you.'



'Sir, I beg your pardon!' be the quick reply from a wither, small,

but not ill-dress old man, 'I only asked--'



'let the lady pass,' say James, peremptorily, wish to save his wife

from annoyance, 'it be of no use, I never look at petition.'



'surely he be not a beggar!' say Isabel, as he draw she on.



'you may be easy about he, my dear,' say James.  'he have lay hold of

Louis, who would swallow the whole spanish legion of impostor. he will

be after we directly with a piteous story.'



Louis be after he, with a face more than half arch fun--'Jem, Jem, it

be your uncle!'



'Nonsense!  how can you be so take in!  don't go and disappoint

granny--I'll settle he.'



'take care, Jem--it be Oliver, and no mistake!  why, he be as like you

as Pendragon blood can make he!  go and beg his pardon.'



James hasten down stair, as Louis bound up--seek Mrs. Frost in

the sitting-room, and, without ceremony, rush up and knock at the

bed-room door.  Jane open it.



'he be come!' cry Louis--'Oliver be come.'



Old Jane give a shriek, and run back wildly, clap her hand.  her

'Here!--in the hall with Jem.'



he fear that he have be too precipitate, for she hide her face in her

those word?  they might be out of taste, but Fitzjocelyn guess that

'really!  Kindred blood signally fail to speak.'

the party go down together in a not very congenial state.

thing go on in this style all the evening.  Oliver have two

there be something strike in his devotion of a lifetime to redeem

he save himself from say--to such a man; but the shrewd, suspicious

the lady, for the first time, do not sit together this morning.

nothing be more intolerable to a man like James than a shower of

the Northwold scholar and master be both rouse to arm in James.

Oliver break out into angry exclamation, and his mother do her utmost

'one breathe freely!' say she, as they leave the house.  'be there

'and that be all you call it?'

'I think you would forgive I for come to meet you under such an

'I care for your opinion as much as ever I do,' say James.



'exactly so,' say Louis, laugh.

you.  that's what I care most about!'



when Louis bade his aunt farewell, he throw his arm round her neck,

look fondly at she, and say, 'dear aunt, you willn't let they tease

you?'



'no, my dear, I be get past be tease,' she say.  'vexation

don't hurt I as much as love do I good, and they'll not forget

their affection.  it be all goodness in Jem, and poor Oliver will

understand it when I have get he into our home way again; but he have

be so long away from home, poor fellow!'



'that's right.  I willn't be uneasy for you.  Squabble as they will, they

willn't hurt you. but, oh!  Dynevor Terrace without you!'



'ah! you must come to I at _home_!'



'Home!  I'm like Jem, jealous for this old house.'



'it be odd how little I feel these thing,' say his aunt.  'if any one

have tell I, when I tear myself away from Cheveleigh, that I should

have it back, how little I should have think that I could take it so

easily!  I wonder at myself when I wake in the morning that I be not

more move by it, nor by leave this dear old place. I suppose it be

because I have not long to stay anywhere.  I can keep nothing in my

head, but that I have get my Oliver!'



'I believe it be the peace that be not of this world!' say Louis.









CHAPTER VII.



ROLAND and OLIVER



    'twa old ancestral pride,

  'twas hope to raise a fall house

    from penury's disgrace,

  to purchase back from usurer

    the birthright of his race.

                       the Lump of Gold--C. mackay.





Mary's letter arrive not long after Louis's return to London; and her

calm, serious, beautifully-express farewell come upon he at last

fail to lessen the weight.



'ah!' say the Earl, when the chief part have be read to he, 'she be

be right you should think no more about she.  after all, the connexion

with that fellow Ponsonby might have be very troublesome, and it be

well, as she say, that it be all over while you be so young.'



'young or old, there be no other Mary in the world,' say Louis, sadly.



'we will say no more about it now.  I understand you, but you will

think differently by-and-by.'



Louis do not answer.  he know that other might have be deceive by

own conviction.  yet he still believe that patience and perseverance

would win the day, and try to derive encouragement and energy from

train he in steadfastness.

meantime, there be a lull at Dynevor Terrace.  Oliver be go to take

'really,' say Charlotte one day, 'I don't think a catastrophe be half

and I never know Mr. James so hard to please.'



Charlotte and Marianne have begin to merge their rivalry in honest

the Merry Wives be parody by the hysterical maid.  Charlotte might

Poor Jane, she be almost grow tart in her partizanship of Oliver.

'that be the only thing to make I not hate it.'



'it be good you should hate it.'



'I do!  why do he come home to bother we?  oh, Jem, cann't I still live

'no, Clara.  the care of granny be your first duty; and during her

the edict be give in stern self-abnegation; but James be very kind

Isabel seldom rouse herself to understand anything about her servant;

and Jane give Charlotte infinite injunction, vary from due care of

'I be faithful!' cry Charlotte.  'I never care for that traitor,

I think that will be proper and scornful.'



'hoity-toity!  that's what she's at!  the good thing you can do too,

Charlotte cry heartily.  no one else be allow that privilege when

James hand his grandmother to her seat in the carriage which be to

'good-bye, my dear,' she say; 'I know the day will come when all this

will be make up.  you know how I have love you both.'



'I wish my uncle all good.'



'I see it now,' she say, hold his hand between both of her.  'it

'ah!'  she shake her head.  'Salome be hard to move.  but you--you be

it be say with that peculiar cheerfulness with which happy old age

can contemplate the end of the pilgrimage, and she look at Louis with

a sunny smile.









CHAPTER VIII.



the restoration.



  when silent time, wi' lightly foot,

    have trod on thirty year,

  I seek again my native land

    Wi' mony hope and fear.

  as I draw near my ancient pile,

    my heart beat a' the way;

  the place I pass seem yet to speak

    of some dear former day.

  some pensy chiel, a new-sprung race,

    Wad next their welcome pay;

    *   *   *   *   *

  but sair on ilka well-ken face

    I miss the youthful bloom.

                         Miss Blamire





Oliver have send order to his mother to sleep in London, and proceed

the next morning by a train which would arrive at about two o'clock.



on that eventful morning, Clara be the prey of Mrs. Beckett, Marianne,

and the french milliner, and in such a flounced glace silk, such a lace

mantle, and such a flowery bonnet be she array, that Lord

Ormersfield bow to she as a stranger, and Louis talk of the

transformation of the Giraffe.  'be it not humiliating,' she say, 'to

be so alter by finery?  you might dress Isabel for ever, and her

nobleness would surmount it all.'



'if you be not the rose, at least you have live near the rose,' say

Louis.  'you don't fall quite short of the character of Miss Dynevor.'



'I wish I be go to school,' say Clara, as they pass along

familiar street; 'then, at least, some one would pity I.'



after two hour spend on the railroad, the train enter a district

with the bleakness, but not the beauty, of the neighbourhood of

mountain; the fresh September breeze be laden with smoke, and

station stand thick upon the line.  as the train dash up to one of

these, a flag be see wave, and the shout of 'Cheveleigh, cheveleigh

road!' greet they.



on the platform stand a tall footman, in the most crimson of coat,

powdered hair, and a stupendous crimson and white shoulder-knot, auch

as Clara have only see go to St. James's.  she would never have

imagine that she have any concern with such splendour; but her

grandmother ask he if the carriage be there, as a mere matter of

course, and Jane devolve on he all luggage care, as coolly as if she

have be rule over he all his life.



as they issue from the station, a thin, uncertain, boyish cheer rang

out, and before they stand a handsome open carriage and four chestnut

horse, with crimson postillion, and huge crimson-and-white satin

rosette.



'willnot they all turn to rat and pumpkin?' whisper Clara to Louis.



'bless the poor boy!' cry Mrs. Frost, between laugh and cry,

'what have he be about?  do he think I be the Sheriff's lady still?'



the party enter the carriage, and the crowd of little boy and girl,

thunder off, with Mrs. Frost apologizing--'Poor Oliver's notion

be on such a grand scale!--He have be so long absent, that he do

not know how much these thing have be disuse.' but no one could look

at her bright tearful eye, and quiver mouth, without see that

she exult in her son's affection and his victory; and after all it

be natural to she, and a resumption of old habit.



they drive through two mile of brown flat heath, with far-away

the engine-house of a mine rise up among shabby building, and

by-and-by be see a square church-tower, with lofty pinnacle, among

which float forth a flag.  the old lady catch hold convulsively of

where your dear grandfather lies!--My last home!'



with brim eye Mrs. Frost gaze on it as it come forth more

distinctly, and Clara look with a sense of awe; but rend she away

from grave thought, shout burst upon her ear, and above they the

pealing crash of all the bell, as they dash under a splendid

and be meet by a party on horseback wave their hat, while a great

hurrah burst out from the number who line the street. Mrs. Frost

bow her thank and wave her hand.  'but oh!' she say, almost

sob, 'where be I?  this be not cheveleigh.'



Lord Ormersfield show she a few old house that they both recognise,

look antiquate in the midst of a modern growth of narrow, conceited

new tenement.  the shouting crowd have, to Fitzjocelyn's eye, more the

aspect of a rabble than of a genuine rejoice peasantry.  what man

there be look beer-attract rather than reputable, and the main

body be whooping boy, woman, nurse-girl, and baby.  the suspicion

cross he that it be a new generation, without memory of forty

year since, wonder rather than welcome, in spite of arch,

bell, and shout.



after another half-mile, a gate swing wide beneath another arch, all

over C. D., the F. studiously omit; and the carriage wheel in amid

a shower of tight little nosegay from a squadron of school-child.

they drive up the long approach, through fir plantation, which draw

from Mrs. Frost a cry of friendly recognition--for her husband have

plant they; but they have not take kindly to the soil, and fifty

year have produce but a starveling growth.  beyond lie an expanse of

parch brown turf, here and there an enclosure of unprosperous tree,

and full in front stand the wide space of stuccoed wall, with a great

gothic window full in the midst, and battlement in the castellate

style of the early year of the nineteenth century.



no one speak.  after the first glance, Mrs. Frost shut her eye to

restrain the hot tear that arise at the thought of the wintry morning,

when ice-drop hang hoary on the fir-tree, as she have drive away from

the portal, whence music be now peal forth a greeting, and where

Oliver be stand on the very spot where, with clenched hand, he have

vow that all should be restore.



alas! how much be in his power to restore?



gaily-dress people surround the entrance, and, amid triumphant

strain from the band, the carriage stop, and Oliver hold out his

hand, say, 'welcome home, mother!'



she leant forward, kiss his brow, and suffer he to lead she up the

step to the hall-door, Lord Ormersfield conduct Clara.  at the door

Mrs. Frost pause, to turn, curtsey, and sign she thank to the throng

who have follow.  her noble aspect and demeanour, so full of dignity

and feeling, obtain a fresh and more genuine acclamation; but

throughout there be a strange sense of unreality; she seem like one

perform a part to gratify her son.  Clara ask her cousin if it

be not like act a play; and it be plain to he that the

spectator beheld it with more curiosity than sympathy.



they be a new race.  property have change hand rapidly in a region

of trade and manufacture, and the old Dynevor name have be forget

past recall, amid the very population who be thrive upon the

identical speculation which have swamp Mr. Frost's fortune.  if the

crowd without look like a mob, the assembly within have a parvenu

appearance; and as Oliver hand his mother across the hall, he

consequence of his guest.



he lead she into a magnificent apartment, all gilding, blue brocade, and

mirror, as far as might be after the model of the day of the

teach Oliver taste either of the eye or of the mind, and his

Dynevor, Lord Ormersfield, Lord Fitzjocelyn,' come so repeatedly as

quite to jingle in their ear.



Sir Andrew Britton, a burly cotton lord, with a wife in all the colour

of the rainbow, seem to be the grand guest.  his lady seat herself

good a neighbourhood it be, and how much pleasure she should have in

in vain do Mrs. Frost look for a face she know, and inquire from her

new acquaintance after familiar old name of place and people.  the

place be either become factory, or some charming new family live

antediluvian; Lady Britton have seldom hear their name, or if any

trace survive, they have never be on her visit list.



at last Oliver come up to she, say, 'here, ma'am, Mr. Henderson

claim an early acquaintance with you.'



'Mr. Henderson!' and she eagerly start up, but look baffled.



'Little George Henderson,' say the grey-head gentleman--for once a

real gentleman--'I assure you I have not forget the happy day I

have spend here.'



'Little George!' as she take he by both hands--'who would have think

it!  you be little George with the apple cheek.  and be no more of

you here?'



he shake his head sadly.  'they would have be even more glad than I

be to welcome you home; they be old, and know you well.'



'ah!  I must learn to ask no question.  and yet, that dear sister

Fanny of yours--'



'go many year since, ma'am.  she die in India.  I hope my daughter

Fanny may put you a little mind of she.'



'be she not here?'



'why, no.  I wish to bring she, but she be but fifteen, and mamma

will not trust she out without herself.  we be quiet people, and the

world be grow too gay for we.'



'Clara and I must come to find you out.  can you believe this tall

creature be poor dear Henry's daughter?' as Clara hasten to greet her

supercilious aside to Lord Fitzjocelyn that the Hendersons be in poor

circumstance, and no one visit they.



'and be no one here whom I know?  not one of the old set, George?'

ask the old lady, mournfully.



'I fear there be hardly any one,' say Mr. Henderson.  'all seem even

to I new people.  stay, do you recollect old Mrs. Golding?'



after a little confusion, Mr. Henderson's old Mrs. Golding prove to be

Mrs. Frost's young Mrs. Golding; and, on the eager inquiry whether she

CHAPTER IX.



the GIANT of the WESTERN STAR.



  come, let we range the subterranean vast,

  Dark catacomb of age, twilight dell,

  and footmark of the century long past,

  which look on we from their sepulchral cell.



  then glad emerge we to the cheer day,

  some sun-range height, or Alpine snowy crown,

  or Chimborazo tower far away

  O'er the great Andes chain, and, look down,

  on flaming Cordilleras, mountain throw

  O'er mountain, vast new realm.

                   the Creation--REV. I. WILLIAMS.





the same impression of the Illustrated London News which delight Jane

Beckett's simple heart in England, cause no small sensation at Lima.



Dona Rosita cast one glance at El Visconde there portray, and then

enormous neighbour, which be both far inland, and of such

CHAPTER X.



the wrong woman in the WRONG PLACE.



  give I again my hollow tree,

  my crust of bread, and liberty.

     the Town Mouse and the Country Mouse--POPE.





the new cook's first compliment to Charlotte be, 'upon my word, you

be a genteel young woman, I dare say you have a lot of sweetheart.'



the indignant denial of the Lady of Eschalott be construe into she

be 'sly,' and Mrs. Cook promise herself to find she out.

he be a devoted and admire husband, but he could not coolly discover
