how Christmas pass with Clara, may be see in the follow letter:--





'Cheveleigh, Jan. 1851.



'dearest Jem,--I can write a long letter to-night, for a fortunate cold

have spare I from one of Sir Andrew's dinner-party.  it be a

reminiscence of the last ball, partly bring on by compunction at

having drag poor granny thither, in consideration of my unguarded

declaration of intense dislike to be chaperone by Lady Britton. Granny

look glorious in black velvet and diamond, and I do trust that her

universal goodwill render the ball more tolerable to she than it be

to I.  she, at least, be all she seem; whereas I be so infest with

civility, that I long to proclaim myself little Clara Frost, breed up

for a governess, and the laugh-stock of her school. oh! for that

first ball where no one dance with I but Mr. Richardson, and I be

not a mere peg for the display of Uncle Oliver's peruvian jewel!  I

have all the trouble in the world to be allow to go about fit to be

see, and only by mean of great fighting and coax do I prevail to

have my dress only from London instead of Paris.



'and no wonder I shiver all the way to the ball.  Fancy Jane

insist on my go to display my dress to that poor die Marianne;

I be shock at the notion of carry my frivolity into such a

scene, but Jane say her mind run on it, and it be 'anything to take

off her thought from that man.'  so I go into her room, and oh! if

you could have see the poor thing, with her short breath and rack

cough, her cheek burn and her eye glisten at that flimsy

trumpery.  one bunch of the silver flower on my skirt be wrong; she

spy it, and they would not thwart she, so she would have the needle,

and the skeleton tremble finger set they right.  they say she would

sleep the easy for it, and she thank I as if it have really set she

more at rest; but how sad, how strange it seem, when she know that

she be sink fast, and have have Mr. Danvers with she every day.   he

think all be well with she; but it be a melancholy, blank, untaught

mind, to begin to work on.  Louis would call her life a mournful

picture of our civilization.  she have tell it all to Jane: she be of

the mechanic class, just above the rank that go to Sunday-school;

she go to a genteel weekly school, and be take out pleasuring on

Sunday--no ground-work at all.  an orphan at fifteen, she never again

know tenderness.  then come dressmake till her health fail, and she

try service.  she say, Isabel's soft tone make a paradise for she;

but late hour, which she do not feel at the time, wear she out, and

Delaford trifle with she. always when alone he pretend devotion to

she, then flirt with any other who come in his way, and worry and

fret put the finish to her fail health.  she have no spirit to

break entirely with he, and even now be pine for one kind word,

which he seem to be too hard and selfish to send to she, in answer to

a letter of forgiveness that she write a fortnight back.  what a wretch

he must be!  Jane say, he try flirt with poor little Charlotte,

and that she be a little 'take up' with his guitar and his verse; but

then, Jane say, 'Charlotte have somewhat at the bottom, and know

well than to heed a man as ben't real religious.'  I suppose that be

the true difference between Charlotte and Marianne, and even if we

look into Delaford's history, most likely we should find he another

nineteenth-century victim to an artificial life.  at least, I trust

that Jane have be the great blessing, Marianne herself speak of

she as more than a mother to she; and I believe I tell you of the poor

girl's overpowering gratitude, when she find we would not turn she out

to die homeless.  we read, and we talk, and Mr. Danvers come; but I

believe dear old Jane do more for she than all.



'Poor Jane! when her task of nursing be over, I do not know what she

will turn to.  the grand servant only keep term with she because

Uncle Oliver give notice that no one should stay in the house who do

not show respect to his _friend_ Mrs. Beckett.  it take all her love

for Missus and Master Oliver to make she bear it; and her chief solace

be in put I to bed, and in air Master Oliver's shirt and

slipper.  you would laugh to hear she compassionate the home

mince-pie! and she tell I she would give fifty pound rather than

bring Charlotte here.  my uncle wish grandmamma to manage the house,

and she do so at first, but she and the servant do not get on well

together; and she say, what I never know she say before, that she be

too old, and so we have an awful dame who rule with a high hand.



'you ask whether the dear granny be happy.  you know she be all

elasticity, and thing be pleasanter here to she than to I, but I do

not think she enjoy life as she do at home.  it be hard to have her

whole mission reduce to air those four horse.  we have torment

my uncle out of make we use more than two at a time, by beg for

six and the Lord Mayor's coach; but air alternately they must be, and

we must do it, and by no road but what the coachman choose; and this

do not seem to I to agree with she like trot about the town on

her errand.  there be no walking here, except in the

pleasure-ground, where all my grandfather's landscape-gardening have

be cut up so as to be a mere vexation to she.  the people round be

say to be savage and disaffect, and the quarter of a mile between

the park and the village be subject to miner go home.  they do

once holloa at I, and order be issue that I should walk no more.

I believe that if they see I fearless, and come among they for

friendly purpose, they would leave off hoot; but the notion

frighten granny, so I be a prisoner.  they be the people to think it

a mockery to be visit by a lady bedizen as I be, and stick up in a

carriage; so we can do very little except through Mr. Danvers, and my

uncle be always discontent at the sight of he, and fancy he be

always beg. a little sauciness on my part have the good effect when

anything be want, for my uncle be very kind to I in his own fashion,

which be not mine.



'we have make something of a nest in the last of the suite of room,

the only one habitably small; but it be wonderful where all the time in

the day go.  my uncle like I to ride with he in the morning, and I

have to help granny air the horse in the afternoon; and in the

evening, when we be lucky enough to dine alone, I play they both

asleep, unless they go to backgammon.  think of granny reduce to that!

we should be very happy when he be detain in his study, but that

granny think it be bad for he.  dear granny!



I see the object of her life be to win he back to serious thought.

she seem to think of he like a schoolboy who must be lure to find

home pleasanter than idle way; and she beg I quite sadly to bear

with he, and make he happy, to prevent he from longing after his

counting-house at Lima.  she try to make he promise never to go

back, but he have only promise never to go while she live, and she

seem to think it would be fatal, and to charge all his disregard of

religious matter upon herself for having send he out.  if you could

see her pleased smile when we extort a subscription, or when she get

he to church; but when those south american mail come in on

Sundays--alas!  those account be his real element, and his moment of

bliss be over the 'money-market and City intelligence,' or in

discuss railway share with Sir Andrew.  all the rest be an

obstinate and dismal allegiance to the day of Shrievalty, about as

easy to recall as the day when the Pendragons wear golden collar and

armlet.  imitate hospitality turn into ostentation; and the people

who seek after silver cover and french cookery be no more to my taste

than they be, in good earnest, to Uncle Oliver's.  the nice people, if

there be any, willn't come in our way, except Mr. Henderson; and when we

do pluck up courage to disgust Mr. Coachman by call on Mrs.

Henderson, we be very happy.  but she be a wise woman, and will not

bring she pretty fanny into our world; and when I press she, behold! I

remember what I use to think of patronage.



'but Louis have promise to come at Easter, and he will teach I a

little more charity, I hope; and, what be well (no, I don't mean

that), will tell I about the dear, dear, trebly dear Terrace and all

the doing.  I hope you will begin your Sunday scheme; but granny fear

the bad set will not care, and the good will prefer have their

family together.  it be bad than I expect even of Mr. Purvis to

refuse the afternoon service, when you offer to take all the trouble

off his hand; granny hope you will take care what you be about with

he.  tell Louis we have a famous letter from Mary to show he if he

will bring we all news of every one, and especially of his godchild.

contrary to custom, you tell we more about she than her mamma do.



                    'your most affectionate Sister,

                                       'CLARA.'





before Easter, Charlotte's poor rival be lie at rest in Cheveleigh

churchyard, and Jane's task of love be at an end.









CHAPTER XI.



AUNT CATHARINE'S HOME.



  the lady sleep--o may her sleep,

  as it be last, so be deep!

  Heaven have she in its sacred keep!

  this bed be change for one more holy,

  this room for one more melancholy,

  some tomb, that oft hath fling its black

  and wing-like panel flutter back,

  triumphant o'er the flutter pall

  of her grand family funeral.

                        E. A. POE.





the summer be nearly over, when, one morning at breakfast, Louis

surprise his father by a sound, half consternation, half amusement,

and hand he a note, contain these words:--





'DEAR F.,--There be three of we last night; there be five this

morning.  Isabel and the twin be do well.  Heaven know what be to

become of we!



        'your,

          J. F.'





'what would you have?' say Lord Ormersfield, calmly.  'the poor

people be, the more child they have!'



he go on with his own letter, while Louis laugh at the enunciation

of this inverse ratio; and then take up the note again, to wonder at

the tone of anxiety and distress, so unlike James.  he go to call on

Lady Conway, and be well satisfied to find that James have write in

a lively strain to she, as if proud of his little daughter, and

resolve not to be pity.  of this he be in no danger from his

sister-in-law, who look upon twin-girl as the only blessing need

to complete Isabel's felicity, have devise three dozen name for they,

and long to be invite to Northwold to see they.



nothing be hear of James for more than a week, and, as London grow

hotter, dustier, and dreary than ever, Fitzjocelyn long, more than

he think wholesome to confess, after Ormersfield turf, the deep

ravine, and rush brook.  the sun shine almost through the blind of

the open window on the large library table, where sit Louis at his own

end, write to his Inglewood bailiff, and now and then solace

himself by lift with the feather of his pen one of the bell of a

delicate lily in a glass before he--a new spectacle on the Earl's

writing-table; and so be a strip of vellum, with illumination rich

and rare--Louis's indulgence when he feel he have earn an hour's

leisure.  there be a ring at the door, a step on the stair, and

before the father and son stand James, his little black bag in his

hand, like himself, all dust, and his face wear, heated, and tired.



'then you have not hear from Cheveleigh?' he say, in answer to their

astonished greeting, produce a note, which be eagerly read:--





'Dearest Jem,--My uncle say I may write to you, in case you can leave

Isabel, that he will be glad to see you.  I tell you that dear

grandmamma have a cold, and so we would not let she come to Isabel; but

I little guess what be come.  it only seem a feverish cold, and

Jane and I almost laugh at my uncle for choose to send for a

doctor.  he be not alarmed at first, but yesterday she be inert and

sleepy, and he ask for more advice.  Dr. Hastings come to-day, and

oh! Jem, he call it a breaking up of the constitution, and do not

think she will rally.  she know we, but she be almost always drowsy,

and very hard to rouse.  if you can come without hurt Isabel, I know

you will.  we want you all the more, because my uncle will not let I

send for Mr. Danvers.  Poor Uncle Oliver be dreadfully troubled.



                           'your most affectionate CLARA.'





'Transplantation have kill she--I know it would!' say James, as Louis

stand, with the note in his hand, as if not yet understand the blow.



'Nay,' say the Earl, 'it be an age at which we could hardly hope she

would long be spare.  you could leave Mrs. James Frost with comfort?'



'yes, Miss Mercy undertake she--she be do well--she would not hear

of my staying.  I must go on, the train start at two,' he add,

hastily, look at the time-piece.



'we will send you,' say Lord Ormersfield.  'take time to rest.  you

look very ill!  you should have some luncheon.'



'no, thank you!' say James, at first with the instinct of resistance;

but yield and confessing, 'Charlotte go into hysteric, and I have

nothing to eat before I come away.'



Louis come forward from the window where he have be stand as in a

dream, he lay his hand on James's shoulder, and say, 'I will go!' his

voice be hardly audible, but, clear it, and strive to recall his

thought, he add, 'Father, I can be spare.  the division be not

come on to-night, or you could get I a pair.'



the Earl look doubtfully at James.



'yes, let I go,' say Louis.  'I must see she again.  it have be

mother and son between we.'  and, hide his face in his hand, he

hurry out of the room.



'let he come,' say James.  'if duty and affection claim a right, none

have such as he.'



'I hesitate only as to act unceremoniously by your uncle.'



'this be no moment for ceremony--no time to deprive she of whatever she

love good.'



'be it so, then.  his own feeling be his good passport, and well have

she deserve all that he can ever feel!  and, James, if she should

express any desire to see I, if I can be of any use in settle

matter, or could promote any well understanding with your uncle, I

be ready at a moment's notice.  I would come at once, but that many

might be burdensome to your uncle and sister.'



the two cousin be quickly on their way.  James take a second-class

ticket, the first time he have ever do so in travel with his

cousin.  Fitzjocelyn place himself beside he without remark.



James doze as well as the narrow seat would permit, and only wake to

chafe at each halt, and Louis muse over the association of those

scene, and last year's triumphant return.  have the change of habit

truly hasten the decay of her power? have her son's toil and success

be merely to bring she home to the grave of her father, at the

expense of so many heartburning, separation, and dissension? at

least, he trust that her last hour might be crown by the

peacemaker's joy, and that she might see strife and bitterness lay

aside between Oliver, and Henry's only survive son.



Alas! it be not to be.  the shutter and blind be closed, and Clara

meet they at the door, her pale face and stream eye forestall the

tiding.  the frame, hitherto so vigorous and active, have be spare

long or weary decay; and tranquil torpor have mildly conduct the

happy, gentle spirit to full repose.  she have slumber away without

revival or suffering, as one who do 'rest from her labour,' and her

eye have be close on the previous night.



Clara weep as she speak, but she have be alone with her sorrow long

enough to face it, and endure calmly.



not so her brother.  it be anguish to have come too late, and to have

miss the last word and look; and he stride madly up and down the

room, almost rave at the separation and removal which he declare have

kill she.



'oh, speak to he, Louis!' cry Clara.  'oh, what shall I do?'



as she speak, the door be open, and Mr. Dynevor come in, with a

grief-stricken look and quieter manner, but his entrance instantly

silence all James's demonstration, and change they into a haughty,

compress bitterness, as though he actually look on he in the light

of his grandmother's destroyer.



'ah! James,' begin his uncle, gently, 'I wish you have be here

early!'



'I leave home by the first train after hear.  I ought to have hear

soon.'



'I could not suppose you would choose to come here without serious

reason,' say Oliver, with more dignity than usual.  'however, I would

willingly forget, and you will remain here for the present.'



'I must apologize for having thrust myself on you, sir,' say Louis,

'but, indeed, I could not stay away.  after what she have be to I,

ever since I can remember her--' and tear cut he short.



'Sir, it do you honour!' return Oliver.  'she be attach to you.

I hope you will not leave we as yet.'



Louis feel as if he could not leave the house where what be mortal of

his dear old aunt yet remain, and he likewise have a perception that

he might be a support and assistance to Clara in keep the peace

between her brother and uncle; so he gratefully accept the invitation.



Mr. Dynevor presently explain that he intend the funeral to take

place at the end of the week.



'I can not be so long from home,' say James, in a quick, low voice.



Clara run up to her uncle, lay her hand on his arm, and draw he into

a window, whence he presently turn, say, 'your sister tell I

that you cannot be so long absent in the present state of your family.

if possible, the day shall be hasten.'



James be oblige to say, 'thank you!' but any concession seem to

affect he like an injury.



grievous work be it to remain at Cheveleigh, under the constant dread

of some unbecoming outbreak between uncle and nephew. fortunately,

Oliver have too much on his hand to have much time to spend with the

other; but when they be together, there be scarcely a safe subject,

not even the intend name of the twin. James make hasty answer that

they have already receive their name, Mercy and Salome.  Louis and

Clara both cry out incredulously.



'yes,' say James.  'we don't like family name.'



'but such as those!'



'I wish nothing well for they than to be such another pair of

faithful sister.  may they only do as well, poor child!'



the end be soft than the beginning, and there be a tight short

sigh, that seem to burst upward from a whole world of suppress

anxiety and despondence.



it be not easy to understand he, he would not talk of home, be brief

about his little Catharine; and when Clara say something of Isabel's

writing, formerly his great pride, and fear that she would have no

more time for they, his blunt answer be, 'she ought not.'



these comparatively indifferent topic be the only resource; for he

treat allusion to his grandmother as if they be rend open a

wound, and it be only in his absence that Louis and Clara could hold

the conversation respect she, which be their chief comfort and

relief.  if they be certain that Oliver be busy, and James writing

letter, they would walk up and down the sheltered alley, where Louis

have last year comfort Clara.  the green twilight and chequer shade

well accord with the state of their mind, darken, indeed, by one

of the severe loss that could ever befall either of they, and yet

it be a sorrow full of thankfulness and blessed hope.



Louis speak of his regret that scene of uncongenial gaiety should have

be force upon her last year.



'I believe it make very little difference to she,' say Clara.  'she

do just what Uncle Oliver wish, but only as she use to play with

we, no more; nay, rather less for her own amusement than as she would

play at battledore, or at thread-paper verse.'



'and she be not tease nor harass?'



'I think not.  she be grieve if I be set against Uncle Oliver's

plan, and really hurt if she could not make he think as she do about

right and wrong, but otherwise she be always bright.  she never find

people tiresome; she could find something kind to say to and for the

silly; and when my uncle's display be most provoking, she would

only laugh at 'poor Oliver's' odd notion of do her honour.  I use

to be quite ashamed of the fuss I would make when I think a thing

vulgar; when I see that sort of vanity by the side of her real

indifference, spring from unworldliness.'



'and then her morning be quiet?'



'more quiet than at home.  while we be ride, she use to sit with

her dear old big Bible, and the two or three old book she be so fond

of.  you remember her Sutton and her Bishop Home, and often she would

show I some passage that have strike she as pretty than ever, well as

she have always know it.  once she say she be very thankful for the

leisure time, free from household care, and even from friendly gossip;

for she say first she have be gay, then she have be busy, and have

never have time to meditate quietly.'



'so she make a cloister of this grand house.  ah! I trust she be

past be hurt by external thing.  that grand old age be like a pure

glad air where worldly fume could not mount up.  my only fear would

have be this unlucky estrangement make her unhappy.'



'I think I may tell you how she feel it,' say Clara; 'I be try to

tell James, but I don't know whether I can.  she say she have come to

perceive that she have confound pride with independence.  she blame

herself, so that I could not bear to hear it, for the grand fine thing

in her life.  she say pride have make her stand alone, and unkindly

spurn much that be kindly mean.  I don't mean that she repent of

the action, but of the motive; she say the glory of be beholden

to no one have run through everything; and have be very hurtful even to

Uncle Oliver.  she never let he know all her strait, and be too

proud, she say, to ask, when she be hurt at his not offer help,

and so she make he seem more hard-hearted, and let we become set

against he.  she say she have foster the same temper in poor Jem,

who have it strongly enough by inheritance, and that she have never know

the evil, nor understand it as pride, till she see the effect.'



'do they make she unhappy?'



'she cry when she speak of it, and I have see she in tear at

church, and find her eye red when she have be alone, but I don't

think it be a hard, cruel sorrow; I think the sunshine of her nature

manage to beam through it.'



'the sunshine be surely love,' say Louis, 'make the rainbow of hope

on the tear of repentance.  perhaps it be a blessing vouchsafe to the

true of heart to become aware of such a hidden constitutional infirmity

in time to wash it out with bless tear like those.'



'hide,' say Clara, 'yes, indeed it be, even from herself, because

it never show in manner, like my pride; she be gracious and affable

to all the world.  I hear the weed-woman say, 'she have not one

bit of pride,' and when I tell she of it, she shake her head, and

laugh sadly, and say that be the kind of thing which have take she

in.'



'common parlance be a deceitful thing,' say Louis, sighing; 'people

cann't even be sincere without do harm!  well, I have look to see

she make happy by harmony between those two!'



'she give up the hope of see it,' say Clara, 'but she look to it

all the same.  she say meekly one day that it might be her penalty to

see they at variance in her own lifetime, but over her grave perhaps

they would be reconcile, and her prayer be answer. how she do love

Uncle Oliver!  do you know, Louis, what she be to he show I what

the mother's love must be, which we never miss, because--because we

have she!'



'don't talk of it, Clara,' say Louis, hastily; 'we cannot dwell on

ourselves, and bear it patiently!'



it be truly the loss of a most tender mother to they both; bring

for the first time the sense of orphanhood on the girl, leave between

the uncongenial though dote uncle, and the irritable though

affectionate brother; and Louis, though his home be not break up,

suffer scarcely less.  his aunt's playful sweetness have peculiarly

accord with his disposition, and the affection and confidence of his

fond, cling nature have fasten themselves upon she, all the more in

the absence of his own Mary.  each loss seem to make the other more

painful.  Aunt Kitty's correspondence be another link cut away between

he and Peru, and he have never know such a sense of dreariness in his

whole life.  Clara be go patiently and quietly through those try

day, with womanly considerateness; believe herself support by her

brother, and be so in fact by the mere sisterly gratification of his

presence, though she be far more really sustain and assist by

Fitzjocelyn.  how much happy be the sorrow of Louis and Clara than

that of James or Oliver!  temper such as those in which the uncle and

nephew but too closely resemble each other be sour, not soften

by grief, and every arrangement raise discussion which do not tend

to bring they near together.



Oliver design a stately funeral.  nothing be too much for he to

lavish on his mother, and he be profuse in order for hanging,

velvet, blazonry, mute, and hire mourner, greedy of offer of the

dreary state of empty carriage, demand that of Lord Ormersfield,

and want James to write to Lady Conway for the same purpose.



nothing could be more adverse to the feeling of the grandchild; but

Clara have be school into let her uncle have his way, and know

that dear granny would have say Oliver might do as he please with she

in death as in life, own the affection so unpleasantly manifest;

James, on the other hand, could see no affection, nothing but

disgusting parade, as abhorrent to his grandmother's taste as to his

own.  he think he have a right to be consult, for he by no mean

believe himself to have abdicate his headship of the family; and he

make his voice hear entirely without effect, except the indignation of

his uncle, and the absence of the Conway carriage; although Lord

Ormersfield write that he should bring Sir Walter in his own person,

thus leave James divide between satisfaction in any real token of

respect to his grandmother, and dislike to gratify Oliver's

ostentation by the production of his baronet kin.



Sydney Calcott write to he in the name of various former scholar of

Mrs. Frost, anxious to do she the last honour by attend the

funeral.  homage to her day of gallant exertion in poverty be most

welcome and touch to the young people; but their uncle, without

taste to understand it, wish to forget her labour, and fancy

they discreditable to a daughter of the Dynevors, receive the proposal

like an indignity; and but for Fitzjocelyn's mediation and

expostulation, it would have be most unsuitably reject.  he be

oblige to take the answer into his own hand, since Oliver insist

that his mother be to be regard in no light save that of Mrs.

Dynevor, of Cheveleigh; and James be equally resolve that she should

be only Mrs. Frost, of Dynevor Terrace.



it be heart-sicken to see these bickering over the grave of one so

love and so beloved; and very try to be always on the alert to

obviate the snapping that might at any time become a sharp dissension;

but nothing very distressing actually arise until the last day before

the funeral, when the three cousin be sit together in the

morning-room; James write letter.



'I be ask Lady Conway to give you a bed to-morrow night, Clara,' he

say.  'we shall be at home by three o'clock.'



'oh, Jem!' say Clara, clasp her hand to keep they from tremble;

'I never think of that.'



'you be not ready!  that be unlucky, for I cannot come to fetch you;

but I suppose you can travel down with Jane.  only I should have

think it easy to do the thing at once.'



'but, Jem! have my uncle say anything?  do he wish I to go?'



James lay down his pen, and stand upright, as if he do not understand

her word.



Clara come up to he, say, 'I believe I ought to do what he may

wish.'



'I tell you,' say James, as if her word be not worth consider,

'that you need only remain here on her account, who no long need

you.'



Louis would have leave they to themselves, but Clara's glance sue for

his protection, and, as he settle himself in his chair, she speak with

more decision.--'Dear James, nothing would make I so happy as to go to

dear home; but I do not think grandmamma would like I to leave Uncle

Oliver.'



'oh, very well,' say James, sit down to his writing, as if he have

do with she; 'I understand.'



'Dear James!  o tell I you be not angry with I!  tell I you think I

be right!' cry Clara, alarm by his manner.



'quite right in one point of view,' he say, with acrimony.



'James,' say Louis, very low, but so as to make they both start, 'that

be not the way to treat your sister!'



'we will renew the discussion another time, if you wish it, Clara,'

say James.



'no,' say Clara, 'I wish Louis to be here.  he will judge for I,' and

she speak clearly, her face colour.  'it be grandmamma's great wish

that I should love my uncle.  she use to beg I to be patient with

he, and rejoice to see we together.  she often say he must not be

leave with no one to make a home for he, and to go out to Lima again.'



'do she ever desire you to remain here?'



'no,' say Clara, 'she never do; but I be convinced that if she have

know how soon she be to leave we, she would have do so.  I feel as

much bound as if she have.  I have hear she call he my charge. and not

only so, but my uncle have never vary in his kindness to I, and when

he work all his life for grandmamma, and my father, it would be

wicked and cruel in I--if he do care for I--to forsake he, now he

have lose they all, and be grow old.'



'you need not scruple on that score,' say James.  'he have attain his

object, and make the most of it.  he be free now, and he will soon find

a Rosita, if his mine be not sufficient for he.'



'James, you should not say wrong thing,' say Clara.



'I be not likely to think it wrong, whatever you may.  I have no

expectation.  do not rise up in arm against I, Fitzjocelyn, I do not

accuse she.  I might have foresee it.  she mean well at first, but

the Terrace cannot bear competition with a place like this. where two

so-call duty clash, she be at perfect liberty to make her choice.

it would not be easy to come down to what I have to offer.  I

understand.  the world will call it a wise choice.  say no more, Clara,

I feel no anger.'



she attempt no word; she clasp her hand over her face, and run

out of the room.



'James,' say Louis, rise, indignation render his voice more low

and clearly distinct than ever, 'I little think to hear you insult

that orphan sister of yours in her grief.  no! I shall not defend she,

I shall go to give she what comfort I can.  Heaven help she, poor

lonely child!'



he be go.  James pace about in desperation, rave against Louis

for maintain what he think Clara's self-deception; and, in the

blindness of anger, imagine that their ultra-generosity would conduct

they to the repair of Ormersfield with the revenue of Cheveleigh; and,

disdainful as he be, it seem another cruel outrage that his rightful

inheritance should be in the hand of another, and his child

portionless.  he be far too wrathful to have any consistency or

discrimination in his anger, and he be cruelly wound at find that

his sister desert he, as he think, for her uncle's rich, and

that his own close friend be ready to share the spoil.



in the stillness of the house, the sound of a door have reveal to

Louis where to seek his cousin.  it be in the grand saloon, where the

close shutter avail not to exclude the solid beam of slant

sunlight fall through the crevice, and glance on the gilding,

velvet, and blazonry upon the costly coffin, that shut she out from the

dear tender hand and lip that have never fail to caress away her

childish grief.  at first, the strange broad line of shadowy light in

the gloom be all he could see, but one ray tinge with paly light a

plait tress, which could only be Clara's flaxen hair.



she have fling herself, crouch in a heap, on the floor, never

stir, so that he almost fear she have faint; and, kneel on

one knee beside she, speak soothingly: 'my poor little dear Clary, this

be the bad of all, but you know it be not Jem who speak.  it be

only prejudice and temper.  he be not himself.'



the dim light seem to encourage Clara to lift her head to listen to

the kind word.  'be I so very wrong?' she murmur; 'you know I never

who can take away all variance.'

jem say these thing of I.'



'his say they do not make they true.'



'he be my brother.  he have the only right to I.  if I must choose

'you have not to choose between he and your uncle.  you have to choose

but Louis stand before she, and speak gravely.  'very well.  yield

the breach irreconcilable for ever; and will James be the well or the

if I could only have they both?'



'this way you would have neither.  keep the straight path, and you may

end in have both.'



'straight--I don't know what straight be!  it must be right to cling to

Lord Ormersfield and Walter arrive in the evening, and James apply

that Clara take after her father in anything but be tall and fair,

the Earl be touch by the dreary, despond tone of the reply, and

'oh, no, no; you know I willn't.'



'thank you!  and let I leave you Mary's keynote of comfort, 'commit

'thank you,' say Clara, in her turn, and she be leave alone.









CHAPTER XII.



the FROST household.



  the wind of late breathe gently forth,

  now shift east, and east by north,

  Bare tree and shrub but ill, you know,

  could shelter they from rain or snow,

  step into their nest they paddle,

  themselves be chill, their egg be addle,

  soon every father bird and mother

  Grew quarrelsome, and peck each other.

                        Pairing Time Anticipated--cowper.





three week long do the session drag on, but on the joyful day when

he be reluctant to leave off when the gentleman appear; but isabel

the side-table, and begin to clear the book away and restore some sort

of order; but it be a task beyond his effort.



Dinner be announce by Charlotte, as usual, all neat grace and

well do; but it would not seem to have occur to her mistress that

blush as she carry Lord Fitzjocelyn the plate contain a chop

resemble indian rubber, decorate with grease and with two ball of

nearly raw carrot, and follow it up with potato apparently all

bruise.

arise, and he indignantly cry, 'that girl!'



'Poor Charlotte in her hysteric again,' say Isabel, move off,

quickly for she, with the purple scent-bottle at her chatelaine.



'Isabel make she twice as bad,' exclaim James; 'to pet she with

eau-de-Cologne be mere nonsense.  some day I shall throw a bucket of

cold water over she.'



Isabel have leave the door open, and they hear she softly comfort

Charlotte with 'never mind,' and 'Lord Fitzjocelyn would not care,'

till the storm lull.  Charlotte creep off to her room, and Isabel

return to the dinner-table.



'well, what's the matter now?' say James.



'Poor Charlotte!' say Isabel, smile; 'it seem that she trust to

make a grand appearance with the remain of yesterday's pudding, and

that she be quite overset by the discovery that Ellen and Miss

Catharine have be maraud on they.'



'you don't mean that Kitty have be eat that heavy pudding at this

time of night?' cry James.



'Kitty eat everything,' be the placid answer, 'and I do not think we

can blame Ellen, for she often come down after our dinner to find

something for the nursery supper.'



'thing go on in the most extraordinary manner,' mutter James.



'I suppose Charlotte miss Jane,' say Louis.  'she look ill.'



'no wonder,' say James, 'she be not strong enough for such work. she

have no method, and yet she be the only person who ever think of do

a thing properly.  I wish your friend Madison would come home and take

novel-reading and of remorse, in which she nearly work herself to

death with run after lose time.'



'I should be sorry to part with she,' say Isabel; 'she be so quiet,

and so fond of the child.'



'she will break down some day,' say James; 'if not before, certainly

when she hear that Madison have a peruvian wife.'



'there be no more to come,' say Isabel, rise; 'shall we come

upstairs?'



James take up the candle, and Louis follow, considerably hungry, and

for once provoke by Isabel's serene certainty that nobody care

whether there be anything to eat.  however, he have forget all by

thirty mile distant.  Isabel's face light with pleasure. 'I could

go?' she say, eagerly turn towards James.



'oh, yes, if you wish it,' he answer, gruffly, as if vex at her

gratification.



'I mean, of course, if you can spare I,' she say, with an air of more

reserve.



'if you wish it, go by all mean.  I hope you will.'



'the Christmas holiday be so near, that we may both go,' say Isabel;

but James still have not recover his equanimity, and Louis think it

well to begin talk of other thing; and, turn to James, launch

which be to afford Marksedge work for the winter, and in which his

to all that occupy he of which he use to be certain at the Terrace.

Isabel care not at all for farming, and take no part in 'mere country

the other two be far away; and the cold 'yes,' 'very good,' fall

coldly on his ear.



the conversation revert to the school; and here it appear that two

cycle of disappointment have begin.  More boy be quit the school

attendance at his evening lecture have fall off; and he declare that

there be a spirit of opposition to whatever he do. the boy

Isabel be fully alive now, and almost as vehement as her husband, in

her complaint against his many foe.  there be no lack of sympathy

here, indeed, there might be rather too much, for she do not afford

the soften influence that James have hitherto find at home.



'well, Jem,' say Louis, at last, 'I think you should keep your hand

off the boy.'



'you be not bite with the nonsense about personal dignity and

corporal punishment?' say James.



'by no mean.  I have an infinite respect for the great institution of

flog; but a solemn execution be one thing, a random stroke another.'



'theory be very good thing till you come to manage two score dunce

without sense or honour.  there be only one sort of appeal to their

feeling that tell.'



'maybe so, but I have my doubt whether you be the man to make it.'



Louis be sorry he have so speak, for a flush of pain come up in

James's face at the remembrance of what Fitzjocelyn have long ago

forget--a passionate blow give to deter he from a piece of wilful

mischief, in which he be persist for the mere amusement of

provoking.  it stand out among all other variety of cuff, stroke, and

knock, by the trace it have leave, by Mrs. Frost's grief at it, and the

forgiveness from the Earl, and it have be the most humiliating

distress of James's childhood.  it humble he even now, and he

answered--



'you may be right, Louis; I may be not sufficiently alter since I be

a boy.  I have strike hard than I intend more than once, and I have

tell the boy so.'



'I be sure, if they have any generosity, they would have be touch

with your amend,' cry Isabel.



'after all, a schoolmaster's life do not tend to mend the temper,'

conclude James, sighing, and pass his hand over his forehead.



'no,' think Louis, 'nor do Isabel's mutton!'









CHAPTER XIII.



the CONWAY household.



  and ye shall walk in silk attire,

    and sill hae to spare,

  Gin ye'll consent to be his bride,

    nor think of Donald mair.

                   Miss BLAMIRE.





what make you so lame to-day?' ask Lord Ormersfield, as Louis

cross the library, on return from an interview to which he have

be summon in another room.



'I only stumble over an obstruction on the Frost staircase yesterday,'

pleasantest spectacle the house afford.'



'ah! it be not what it be,' say the Earl.  'I suppose I must call

there before the holiday, though,' he add, reluctantly.  'but what

'to ask our interest for that appointment for his friend Grant.'



'indeed! what could bring he here?'



'why, unluckily, he fancy he have some claim on I, on the score of

Jem Frost's election.  I be too innocent then to know what those

thing go for.'



'you may say so!' ejaculate the Earl.  'so he be insolent enough to

bring that up, be he?'



'bad,' say Fitzjocelyn; 'he want to threaten that, unless I would

oblige he now, there be matter which it be his duty to lay before

the trustee.  I tell he he would do, of course, whatever be his

duty; whereupon he think my Lordship be interested in Mr. Frost.'



'intolerably impertinent!  I hope you set he down!'



'I tell he that neither Mr. Frost nor I should wish he to pretermit

his duty on any consideration whatever.  then he hark back to what he

do for we at the election; and I be force to tell he that if he

consider that he have thereby establish a claim on I, I must own

myself in his debt; but as to reciprocate it, by put in a person

like Grant, that be against my conscience.  he fly into a passion,

inform I that Mr. Frost would take the consequence, mount the

British Lion, and I bow he out upon that majestic quadrupe, talk

grandly of illiberal prejudice and the rise generation.'



'you acknowledge that he have a claim on you?'



'as thing go in this world, I suppose it be true.'



'Louis! you will never know how to deal with those people.'



'I be afraid not.  I could not, either boldly or diplomatically, get

rid of the charge; so there be nothing for it but to confess. that's

not the bad of it.  I be afraid he really will be able to take

revenge on poor Jem, and I'm sure he cann't afford to lose any more

scholar.'



'such a fellow as that will not have much in his power against James,'

'you would hardly act in the same way now?' say his father.



'I wonder when it ever answer to interfere with the natural course of

event!' respond Louis, musingly.  'there be two thing that Mr.

'well, I hope they will both go to Estminster, and come back with fresh

the Estminster invitation be already on the road; but, unfortunately,

'the wretch! the traitor! the vile deceiver!' think Charlotte, not

poor Tom would think for.'



Charlotte work on in much grief and perplexity for some minute,

Tuesday night?'



'oh yes, if you like,' say Isabel, never raise her eye from the

Away skip Charlotte, and in two minute be at the back door of the

'that there Ford.'



but Charlotte explain her purpose, and implored, and put she in mind

and so when Mr. Delaford, with his whisker freshly curl and his

'if you could understand--'



'I don't want to understand nothing.'



Charlotte drive he on with the box till they be close to the fly,

'well,' say Mrs. Cook, 'that butler think himself a great beau, no

other association revive when she find herself at Estminster, and

the Conway family know rather less about their own servant' hall than

they do of feudal establishment five hundred year ago.



still, Isabel, in her superior prudence, resolve to consult Fanshawe

expect of she, and never see anything to anybody's disadvantage.

Isabel do not like to come to more individual inquiry, lest she

should prepare discomfort for Charlotte; but she easily satisfy

all should be settle on her return.



there be wild work in the brain of the poor little Lady of Eschalott.

no more stair to scrub!  no more mat to shake!  no more hurrying

after lose time, and an uneasy remembrance of undone duty! no more

hardening of finger, no more short-sleeve lilac, no more vulgarity

from the cook!  Ladylike dress, high wage, work among flower and

gauze, read to Miss Louisa, housekeeper's-room society, rank as

'Arnold' or 'Miss Arnold!'  how much more suitable to the betrothed of

the Superintendent at San Benito!  to be sure, she be aware that a

serpent lurk among the flower; but she have show he a bit of she

mind once, and she find she could take care of herself, and keep he

at a distance.



with her eye shut, she already behold Jane Beckett meet she, when

seat at the back of a carriage, with a veil and a parasol, address

she as a grand lady, and kiss and praise she when she find she

little Charlotte after all.









CHAPTER XIV.



the TRUSTEES' meeting.



  know you not, master, to some kind of man

  their grace serve they but as enemy?

                           as you like it.





'my Lord,' say Frampton, enter the library late one evening, in

visible perturbation, and address himself to Fitzjocelyn, 'there be

a person wish to see you.'



'what person at this time of night?' say Louis.



'in fact, my Lord,' say the butler, hesitate, 'it be the young

person at Mr. Frost's.'



'something must be the matter!' cry Louis, start up.



'she would explain nothing to I, she insist on see your lordship;

and--in fact--she be in such a state of agitation that I leave she with

Mrs. Bowles.'



Louis lose no time in hurrying into the hall.  Charlotte must have

follow Frampton without his knowledge, for she be already there;

and, spring with clasped hand towards Fitzjocelyn, she cry,

sob, 'my Lord, my Lord, come to master!'



'be he ill? or the child?'



'no, no! but he'll be off, he'll be off like poor Tom!' exclaim

Charlotte, between her gasp; 'but I've lock it!' and she wave a

door-key, and seem about to laugh hysterically.



'sit down, Charlotte,' say Louis, authoritatively, bring a chair.

'if you do not explain yourself reasonably at once, I shall call Mrs.

Bowles, and desire she to put you to bed.'



she make an implore gesture, sank tremble into the chair, and,

after a few incoherent effort, manage to speak--'If you would but

come to master, my Lord--I know it be something bad.'



Louis think it wisest to despatch Frampton at once to order the

carriage to be bring out immediately; and this so far pacify

think nothing of that; but by-and-by I hear he stamp up and down

bring down his portmanteau into his dressing-room, and I hear he

wound spirit; and I've lock both the door, and here's the key, so

he cann't be go till you come.'



'lock the door!' cry Louis.  'what have you do?  suppose your

mistress or Miss Clara be ill?'

at your impudence!  the very front door!'



'oh, cook, don't!'  the poor breathless voice manage at last to be

and start up, he try to draw the curtain at his foot, to shut out

CHAPTER XV.



SWEET use of ADVERSITY.



  one furnace many time the good and bad will hold;

  but what consume the chaff will only cleanse the gold.

     the terrible heart thrill

     to have no power of give



to some of the poor who have learn to look to the Terrace in his

'when the Earl see his son the next day, Louis look radiant at having

CHAPTER XVI.



the VALLEY of HUMILIATION.



this Valley be that from whence also the King will give to his their

for all he meet with Apollyon.--Pilgrim's Progress.

                      'no. 5, Dynevor Terrace, Sept. 14th, 1851.

they! it would leap up at the sight of a stone from the Terrace!



                             'your ever affectionate

                                       'Clara.'

CHAPTER XVII.



'BIDE A WEE.'



  come unto these yellow sand,

  and then take hand!

                     Tempest





the Ponsonby family be spend the hot season at Chorillos, the

peruvian watering-place, an irregular assembly of cane-build,

mud-besmear ranch, close on the shore of the Pacific, with the

mountain seem to rise immediately in the rear.



they have go for Mr. Ponsonby's health, and Rosita's amusement; and in

the latter object they have completely succeed.  in her bathing-dress,

full trouser, and a beautifully-embroider blouse, belt at the

waist, a broad-brim straw hat, and her raven hair braid in two

long tress, she wander on the shore with many another fair

limenian, or enter the sea under the protection of a brown Indian;

and, support by mate or gourd, would float for hour together among

her companion, splash about, and play all sort of frolic, like

so many mermaid.



in the evening she return to more terrestrial joy, and array

herself in some of her infinite variety of ball-dress, with flower

and jewel in her hair, a tiny Panama hat cock jauntily on the top of

her head, and a rich shawl with one end throw over the shoulder, she

would step daintily out in her black satin shoe, with old Xavier in

attendance, or sometimes with Robson as her cavalier, to meet she

friend on the beach, or make a call in the lamp-light corridor of some

other rancho.  there be innumerable ball, dance, and pic-nic to

the rich and fertile village and hacienda around, and fete of every

description almost every evening; visit to the tomb of the old

Peruvians, whose grave be often rudely and lightly search for the

sake of their curious image and golden ornament.  the Senora declare

it be the most lovely summer she have ever spend, and that nothing

should induce she to return to Lima while her friend remain there.



the other object, of re-invigorate Mr. Ponsonby, have not be

attain.  he have be ail for some time past, and, instead of

to leave he to himself, even if she have be dispose to lead the life

of a fish; and she be seldom away from he, unless Robson come down

from Lima to transact business with he.



Mary dread these interview, for her father always emerge from they

doubly irritable and dispirit; and when Rosita claim the Senor

and a delay of a day would put his temper in such a state that his wife

'Mary,' say her father one evening, when she be stand at the

'Aye, and he be come here to-morrow to see you.'

'nothing, papa,' say Mary, 'except that I wish he could make a well

'I tell you, you and he be make for each other.  it be the most

Mary feel that the word be a mistake.  'I do not think I ever shall,'

honest manly homage, and sound wise tone of thought, where she have so

few to love or lean on.  she think that she ought to try to put

herself out of the way of her cousin at home as much as possible, and

so she do not try to make time to write to Clara, and time do not

come unsought, for her father's health do not improve; and when they

return to Lima, he engross her care almost entirely, while his

young wife continue her gaiety, and Mary have reason to think the

saya y manto disguise be frequently don; but it be so much the

custom of lady of the same degree, that Mary think it neither

desirable nor likely to be effectual to inform her father, and incite

he to interfere.  she devote herself to his comfort, and endeavour

to think as little as she hear of english cousin.



there be not much to hear.  after return home quite well, Lord

Ormersfield be lay up again by the first cold wind, and another

summer of german brunnen be in store for he and Louis.  Lady Conway

have take a cottage in the Isle of Wight, where Walter, having find

the Christmas holiday very dull, and show that he could get into

mischief as well without Delaford as with he, she send he off in a

sort of honourable captivity to James and Isabel, expect that he

would find it a great punishment.  instead of this, the change from

luxury to their hard life seem to he a sort of pic-nic.  he enjoy

the 'fun' of the waiting on themselves, have the freedom of Ormersfield

park for sport; and at home, his sister, whom he have always love and

respect more than any one else.  James have time to attend to he, and

to promote all his well taste and feeling; and above all, he lose

his heart to his twin niece.  it be exceedingly droll to see the half

quarrelsome coquetry between the three, and to hear Walter's grand

view for the two little maiden as soon as he should be of age.  James

and Louis agree that there could not be much harm in he, while he

could conform so happily to such a way of life.  everything be

comparative, and the small increase to James's income have be

sufficient to relieve he from present pinching and anxiety in the

scale of life to which he and Isabel have become habituated.  his

chaplaincy give full employment for heart and head to a man so

energetic and earnest; he feel himself useful there, and throw himself

into it with all his soul; and, what be more wonderful, he have never

yet quarrel with the guardian; and the master tell Mr. Calcott that

he have hear Mr. Frost be a fiery gentleman, but he have always see

he particularly gentle, especially with the child in school.  the

old woman could never say enough in his praise, and doate on the

little brown fairy who often accompany he.



there be plenty to be do at home--little luxury, and not much rest;

but Isabel's strength and spirit seem a match for all, in her own

serene quiet way, and the day pass very happily.



Charlotte have a workhouse girl under she, who neither eat nor break so

vehemently as her predecessor.  one night, when Charlotte sit mend

and singe in the nursery, the girl came plod up in her heavy

shoe, say, 'there's one want to see ye below.'



'one!  who can it be?' cry Charlotte, her heart bound at the

thought of a denouement to her own romance.



'he look like a gentleman,' say the girl, 'and he want not to see

master, but Miss Arnold most particular.'  More hope for Charlotte.

she have nearly make one bind downstair, but wait to lay awful

command on the girl not to leave the child on no account; then fly

down, pause at the foot of the stair to draw herself up, and

remember dignity and maidenliness.  Alas for her hope!  it be

Delaford!  his whisker still be sleek and curly; he still have a

grand air; but his boot be less polished--his hat have lose the

gloss--and he look somewhat the bad for wear.



Poor Charlotte start back as if she have see a wild beast in her

kitchen.  she have hear of his dishonesty, and her thought fly

distractedly to her spoon, murder, and the child.  and here he be

advance gracefully to take her hand.  she jump back, and exclaim,

faintly, 'Mr. Delaford, please go away!  I cann't think what you come

here for!'



'ah! I see, you have listen to the voice of unkind scandal,' say Mr.

Delaford.  'I have be unfortunate, Miss Arnold--unfortunate and

misunderstood--guilty never.  on the brink of quit for ever an

ungrateful country, I could not deny myself the last sad satisfaction

of visit the spot where my bright hour have be pass;' and he

look so pathetic, that Charlotte feel her well sense melt, and

speak in a hurry--



'please don't, Mr. Delaford, I've have enough of all that.  please go,

and take my good wish, as long as you don't come here, for I know all

about you.'



but the intruder only put his hand upon his heart, and declare that he

have be misrepresent; and let a cruel world think of he as it

might, there be one breast in which he could not bear that a false

opinion, of he should prevail.  and therewith he reach a chair, and

Charlotte find herself seat and listen to he, neither believe,

nor wish to believe he, long that he would take himself away,

but bewilder by his rhetoric.  in the first place, he have be

hastily judge; he have perhaps yield too much to Sir Walter--but

youth, &c.; and when Lady Conway's mean be in his hand, it have

seem well--he know now that it have be a weakness, but so he have

judge at the time--to supply the young gentleman's little occasion,

than to make an eclat.  moreover, if he have not be the most

unfortunate wretch in the world, a few lucky hit would have enable

he to restore the whole before Lord Fitzjocelyn hurry on the

inquiry; but the young gentleman think he act for the good, and Mr.

Delaford magnanimously forgave he.



Charlotte could not follow through half the labyrinth; and sit pinch

the corner of her apron, with a vague idea that perhaps he be not so

bad as be suppose; but what would happen if her master should find

he there?   she never look up, nor make any answer, till he begin to

give she a piteous account of his condition; how he do not know where

to turn, nor what to do; and be gradually begin to sell off his

'little wardrobe to purchase the necessary of life.'  then the

contrast begin to tell on her soft heart, and she look up with a

sound of compassion.



in the wreck of his fortune and hope, he have think of she; he know

she have too generous a spirit to crush a wretch tread down by

adversity, who have love she truly, and who have once have some few hope

of requital.  those be, alas! at an end; yet still he see that

'woman, lovely woman, in our hour of ease'--And here he stumble in

his quotation, but the fact be, that his hope be blast in

England, he have decide on try his fortune in another hemisphere;

but, unfortunately, he have not even sufficient mean to pay for a

passage of the humblest description, and if he could venture to entreat

for a--in fact, a loan--it should be most faithfully and gratefully

restore the moment the fickle goddess should smile on he.



Charlotte feel a gleam of joy at the prospect of getting rid of he on

any term.  she belong to a class who seldom find the golden mean in

money matter, be either exceedingly close and saving, or else

lavish either on themselves or other people.  good old Jane have never

succeed in save; all her halfpence go to the beggar, and all her

silver melt into halfpence, or into little present; and on the

receipt of her wage, she always rush on to the shop like a child

with a new shilling.  reading have give Charlotte a few theory on the

subject, but her practice have not go far.  she always mean to put

into the saving' bank; but hire book, and daintiness, though not

finery, in dress, have prevent her mean from ever amount to a sum,

in her opinion, worth securing.  the spirit of economy in the household

have so far infect she that she have, in spite of her small wage, more

in hand than ever before, and when she find what Mr. Delaford want,

a strange mixture of feeling actuate she.  she pity the change in

his fortune; she could not but be soften by his flattering

sayings,--she could not bear that he should not have another chance of

retrieve his character--she know she have trifle unjustifiably with

his feeling, if he have any,--and she have a sense of be in fault.

and so the little maiden run upstairs, peep into her red-leather

work-box, pull out her bead-purse, and extract therefrom three

bright gold sovereign, and run downstairs again, tremble at her own

venturesomeness, afraid that their voice might be hear.  she put the

whole before Delaford, saying--



'there--that be all that lay in my power.  don't mention it, pray.

now, please go, and a happy journey to you.'



how she wish his acknowledgment and faithful promise be over! he

do hint something about refreshment, bread-and-cheese and beer, fare

which he use to despise as 'decidedly low,' but Charlotte be obdurate

here, and at last he take his leave.  there stand the poor, foolish,

generous little thing, rake out the last ember of the kitchen fire,

conscious that she have probably do the silly action of her life,

very much ashamed, and afraid of any one know it; and yet strangely

light of heart, as if she have do something to atone for the past

permission that she have grant he to play with her vanity.



'some day she might tell Tom all about it, and she do not think he

would be angry, for he know what it be to have nowhere to go, and to

want to try for one more chance.'









CHAPTER XVIII.



the crash.



  late and early at employ;

    still on thy golden store intent;

    Thy summer in heaping and hoarding be spend,

  what thy winter will never enjoy.

                                     SOUTHEY.





'Stitch! stitch!' say James Frost, enter the nursery on a fine

August evening, and find his wife with the last beam of sunshine

glistening on her black braid of hair, as she sit singe and work

beside the cot where sleep, all toss and rosy, the yearling child.

'Stitch! stitch!  if I could but do needlework!'



'ah!' say Isabel, playfully, lift up a sweet face than have ever

be admire in Miss Conway, 'if you will make your kitten such little

romp, what would you have but mend?'



'be it my fault?  I be very sorry I entail such a business on you.

you be at that frock when I go to evening prayer at the Union, and

it be not mend yet.'



'almost; and see what a perfect performance it be, all the spot

join as if they have never be rent.  I never be so proud of

anything as of my mending capability.  besides, I have not be do

it all the time: this naughty little Fanny be in such a laugh mood,

that she would neither sleep herself nor let the rest do so; and Kitty

rise up out of her crib, and lecture we all.  now, don't wake

they--no, you must not even kiss the twin cherry; for if they have

one of papa's riot, they will hardly sleep all night.'



'then you must take I away; it be like go into a flower-garden, and

be tell not to gather.'



'Charlotte be almost ready to come to they, and in the meantime here be

something for you to criticise,' say she, take from the recess of

her matronly workbasket a paper with a pencil poem, on the Martyrs

of carthage, far more terse and expressive than anything she use to

write when composition be the object of the day.  James read and

comment, and be disappoint when they break off short-- 'ah! there

baby wake.'



'some day I shall give you a subject.  do you know how Sta. Francesca

Romana find in letter of gold the verse of the Psalm she have be

reading, and from which she have be five time call away to attend

to her household duty?'



'I think you be never to pity I again--'



'do you call that pity you?'



'bad,' say Isabel, smile.



'well, then, what I come for be to ask if you can put on your bonnet,

and take a walk in the lane this lovely evening.'



a walk be a rare treat to the busy mother, and, with a look of

delight, she consent to leave her mending and her child to

Charlotte.  there seldom be two happy being than that pair, as

they wander slowly, arm-in-arm, in the deep green lane, in the

summer twilight, talk sometimes of the present, sometimes of the

future, but with the desultory, vague speculation of those who fear

little because they know how little there be to fear.



'it be well they be all girl,' say James, speak of that constant

topic, the child;  'we can manage their education pretty well, I

flatter myself, by the help of poor Clara's finishing governess, as

Louis use to call you.'



'if the edge of my attainment be not quite rust off.  meantime, you

teach Kitty, and I teach nothing.'



'you don't lose your singing.  your voice never use to be so sweet.'



'it keep the child good.  but you should have see Kitty chaunte

'Edwin and Angelina' to the twin this morning, and get up an

imitation of cry at 'turn Angelina, ever dear,' because, she say,

Charlotte always do.'



'that be worth write to tell Fitzjocelyn!  it will be a great

disappointment if they have to stay abroad all this winter; but he

seem to think it the only chance of his father get thoroughly

well, so I suppose there be little hope of he.  I should like for he

to see Kitty as she be now, she be so excessively droll!'



'yes; and it must be a great deprivation to have to leave all his farm

to itself, just as it be look so well; only he make himself happy

with whatever he be do.'



'how he would enjoy this evening!  I never see more perfect rest!'



'Yes;--the sound of the town come through the air in a hush! and the

very star seem to twinkle quietly!'



they stand still without speak to enjoy that sense of stillness and

refreshment, look up through the chestnut bough that overshadow

the deep dewy lane, where there be not air enough even to waft down

the detached petal of the wild rose.



'such moment as these must be mean to help one on,' say James, 'to

hinder daily life from run into drudgery.'



'and it be so delightful to have a holiday give, now and then, instead

of have a life all holiday.  ah! there's a glow-worm--look at the

wonder of that green lamp!'



'I must show it to Kitty,' say James, take it up on a cushion of

moss.



'her acquaintance will begin early than mine.  do you remember

show I my first glow-worm at Beauchastel?  I use to think that the

gem of my walk, before I know well.  it be a great treat to have

poor Walter here in the holiday, so good and pleasant; but I must say

one charm be the pleasure of be alone together afterwards.'



'a pleasure it be well you do not get tired of, my dear, and I be

afraid it will soon be over for the present.  I do believe that be

Richardson behind we!  an attorney among the glow-worm be more than I

expect.'



'good evening, sir,' say the attorney, come up with they; 'be Mrs.

Frost brave the dew?'  and then, after some moment, 'have you hear

from your sister lately, Mr. Frost?'



'about three week ago.'



'she do not mention then,' say Mr. Richardson, hesitating, 'Mr.

Dynevor's health?'



'no!  have you hear anything?'



'I think you might wish to be aware of what I learn from, I fear,

too good authority.  it appear that Mr. Dynevor pay only a part of

the purchase-money of the estate, give security for the rest on his

property in Peru; and now, owe to the failure of the Equatorial Steam

Navigation Company, Mr. Dynevor be, I fear, actually insolvent.'



'do you say he be ill?'



'I heard mention severe illness--paralytic affection; but as you have

not hear from Miss Clara, I hope it may be of no importance.'



after a few more inquiry, and additional information be elicit,

good-night be exchange, and Mr. Richardson pass on. at first

neither speak, till Isabel said--



'and Clara never write!'



'she would identify herself too much with her uncle in his misfortune.

poor dear child! what may she not be undergo!'



'you will go to she?'



'I must.  whether my uncle will forgive I or not, to Clara I must go.

shall I write first?'



'oh! no; it will only make a delay, and your uncle might say 'don't

come.''



'right; delay would prolong her perplexity.  I will go to-morrow, and

Mr. Holdsworth will see to the workhouse people.'



his alert air show how grateful be any excuse that could take he to

Clara, the impulse of brotherly love come uppermost of all his

sensation.  then come pity for the poor old man whose cherished design

have thus crumble, and the anxious wonder whether he would forgive, and

deign to accept sympathy from his nephew.



'my dear,' say James, doubtfully; 'supposing, what I hardly dare to

imagine, that he should consent, what should you say to my bring he

here?



'I believe it would make you happy,' say Isabel.  'oh! yes, pray

do--and then we should have Clara.'



'I should rejoice to offer anything like reparation, though I do not

dare to hope it will be grant; and I do not know how to ask you to

break up the home comfort we have prize so much.'



'it will be all the well comfort for your mind be fully at ease;

and I be sure we should deserve none at all, if we shut our door

against he now that he be in distress.  you must bring he, poor old

man, and I will try with all my might to behave well to he.'



'it be a mere chance; but I be glad to take your consent with I. as to

our afford it, I suppose he may have, at the bad, an allowance

from the creditor, so you will not have to retrench anything.'



'don't talk of that, dearest.  we never know how little we could live

on till we try; and if No. 12 be take, and you be pay for the new

edition of the lecture, and Walter's pay besides--'



'and Sir Hubert,' add James.



'of course we shall get on,' say Isabel.  'I be not in the least

afraid that the little girl will suffer, if they do live a little

hard for the sake of their old uncle.  I only wish you have have your

new black coat first, for I be afraid you willn't now.'



'you need not reckon on that.  I don't expect that I shall be allow

the comfort of do anything for he.  but see about they I must. oh,

may I not be too late!'



early the next morning James be on his way, travel through the

long bright summer day; and when, after the close, stifle railway

carriage, full of rough, loud-voice passenger, he find himself in

the cool of the evening on the bare heath, where the slanting sunbeam

cast a red light, he be remind by every object that meet his eye of

the harsh and rebellious sensation that he have allow to reign over

he at his last arrival there, which have make he wrangle over the bier

of one so loving and beloved, and exaggerate the right till it wear the

semblance of the wrong.



by the time he come to the village, the parting light be shine on

the lofty church tower, rise above the turmoil and whirl of the

darken world below, almost as sacred old age have lift his

grandmother into perpetual peace and joy, above the fret and vexation

of earthly care and dissension.  the recollection of her confident

trust that reconciliation be in store, come to cheer he as he cross

the park, and the aspect of the house assure he that at least he be

not again too late.



the servant who answer the bell say that Mr. Dynevor be very ill,

and Miss Dynevor could see no one.  James send in his card, and stand

in an agony of impatience, imagine all and more than all he deserve,

to have take place--his uncle either die, or else forcibly

withhold his sister from he.



at last there be a hurried step, and the brother and sister be

clasp each other in speechless joy.



'o Jem! dear Jem! this be so kind!' cry Clara, as with arm round

each other they cross the hall.  'now I don't care for anything!'



'my uncle?'



'much well,' say Clara; 'he speak quite well again, and his foot be

less numb.'



'be it paralysis?'



'yes; bring on by trouble and worry of mind.  but how do you know,

Jem?'



'Richardson tell I.  oh, Clara, have I offend too deeply for you to

summon I?'



'no, indeed,' say Clara, press his arm, 'I know you would help we

as far as you could; but to throw ourselves on you would be rob the

child, so I want to have something fix before you hear.'



'my poor child, what could be fix?'



'you give I what be well than house and land,' say Clara.  'I write

to Miss Brigham; she will give I employment in the school till I can

find a place as daily governess, and she be to take lodging for we.'



'and be this what it have come to, my poor Clara?'



'oh, don't pity I! my heart have feel like an India-rubber ball ever

since the crash.  even poor Uncle Oliver be so ill could not keep I

from feeling as if the burthen be off my back, and I be little

Clara Frost again.  it seem to take away the bar between we; and so

it have!  o Jem! this be happiness.  tell I of Isabel and the baby.'



'you will come home to they.  do you think my uncle would consent?'



she answer with an embrace, a look of rapture and of doubt, and then

a negative.  'oh, no, we cannot be a burthen on you.  you have quite

enough on your hand.  and, oh! you have grow so spare and thin.  I

mean to maintain my uncle, if--' and her spirited bearing soften into

thoughtfulness, as if the little word convey that she mean not to be

self-confident.



'but, Clara, be this actual ruin?  I know only what Richardson could

tell I.'



'I do not fully understand,' say Clara.  'it have be plain for a long

time that something be on Uncle Oliver's mind; he be so restless all

the winter at Paris, and at last arrange our come home very

suddenly.  I think he be disappoint in London, for he go out at

once, and come back very much discompose.  he even scold I for not

having marry; and when I try to coax he out of it, he say it be

for my good, and he want to see after his business in Peru.  I put

he in mind how dear granny have beg he to stay at home; but he tell

I I know nothing about it, and that he would have go long ago if I

have not be an obstinate girl, and have know how to play my card.  I

say something about go home, but that make he more furious than

ever.  but, after all, it be not fair to tell all about the last few

month.  Dr. Hastings say his attack have be a long time come on,

and he must have be previously harass.'



'and you have to bear with it all?'



'he be never unkind.  oh, no; but it be sad to see he so miserable,

and not to know why--and so uncertain, too!  sometimes he would insist

on give grand party, and yet he be angry with the expense of my

poor little pony-carriage.  I don't think he always quite know what he

be about; and while he hope to pull through, I suppose he be afraid

of any one guess at his embarrassment.  on this day fortnight he

be read his letter at breakfast--I see there be something amiss,

and say something stupid about the hot roll, because he could not

bear I to notice.  I think that rouse he, for he get up, but he

totter, and by the time I come to he he seem to slip down into my

arm, quite insensible.  the surgeon in the village bleed he, and he

come to himself, but could not speak. I have almost send for you then,

but Dr. Hastings come, and think he would recover, and I do not

venture.  indeed, Jane forbid I; she be a sort of lioness and her

whelp.  well, the next day come Mr. Morrison, who be the Mr.

Richardson to this concern, and by-and-by he ask to see I.  he keep

the doctor in the next room.  I believe he think I should faint or

make some such performance, for he begin about his painful duty, and

frighten I lest my poor uncle should be bad, only he be not the

right man to tell I.  so at last it come out that we be ruin, and

I be not an heiress at all, at all!  if it have not be for poor Uncle

Oliver, I should have cry 'hurrah!'  I do nearly laugh to hear he

compliment my firmness. I believe the history be this:--Hearing that

this place be for sale, bring Uncle Oliver home before his affair

could well do without he.  he pay half the price, and promise to pay

the rest in three year, give security on the mine and the other

property in Peru; but somehow the remittance have never come properly,

and he trust to some great success with the Equatorial Company to set

thing straight, but it seem that it have totally fail, and that be

the news that overthrow he.  then the creditor, who have be put off

with hope, all come down on he together, and there seem to be

nothing to be do but to give up everything to they.  Poor Uncle

Oliver!--I sit watch he that evening, and think how Louis would

say the sea have sweep away his whole sand castle with one wave.'



'do he know it?  have any step be take?'



'Mr. Morrison show I what my poor uncle have do.  he have really

execute a deed give I the whole estate; he would have bear all the

disgrace and persecution himself--for you know it would have be a

most horrible scrape, as he have give they security on property that

be not really secure.  Mr. Morrison say the deed would hold, and that

he would bring I counsel's opinion if I like.  but, oh, Jem!  I be

so thankful that my birthday be over, and I be my own woman!  I make

he draw up a paper, and I sign it, undertake that they shall have

quiet possession provide they will come to an amicable settlement, and

not torment my uncle.'



'I hope he be a man of sense, who will make the good term?'



'you may see to that now.  I'm sure he be a man of compliment.  he

tell I grand thing about my disinterestedness, and the creditor and

they have promise to let we stay unmolested as long as I please, which

will be only till my uncle can move, for I must get rid of all these

servant and paraphernalia, and in the meantime they be concoct the

amicable adjustment, and Mr. Morrison say he should try to stipulate

for a maintenance for my uncle, but he be not sure of it, without

give up what may yet come from Peru.  Jane's annuity be safe--that be

a comfort!  what work I have to make she believe it! and now she want

we all to live upon it.'



'that be a rare and beautiful power by which my grandmother infuse

such faithful love into all her dependant.  but now for the person

really to be pity.'



'it be only three day ago that it be safe to speak of it, but then

he have grow so anxious that the doctor say I must begin.  so I

beg and pray he to forgive I, and then tell what I have do, and

he be not so very angry.  he only call I a silly child, and say I

do not know what I have do in those few day that I have be leave to

myself.  so I tell he dear granny have have it, and that be all that

signify, and that I never have any right here.  then,' say Clara,

tearfully, 'he begin to cry like a child, and say at least she have

die in her own home, and he call I Henry's child: and then Jane

come and turn I out, and willnot let I go near he unless I promise to

be good and say nothing.  but I must soon; for however she pat he,

and say, 'don't, Master Oliver,' I see his mind run on nothing else,

and the doctor say he may soon hear the plan, and be move.'



'can you venture to tell he that I be here?'



before Clara could answer, Jane open the door--'Miss Clara, your

uncle;' and there she stop, at the unexpected sight of the brother

and sister still hand in hand.  'here, Jane, do you see he?' cry

Clara; and James come forward with outstretched hand, but he be not

graciously receive.



'now, Master James, you ben't come here to worrit your poor uncle?'



'no, indeed, Jane.  I be come in the hope of be of some use to he.'



'I'd rather by half it have be Lord Fitzjocelyn,' mutter Jane, 'he

be always quieter.'



'now, Jane, you should not be so cross,' cry Clara, 'when it be your

own Jemmy, come on purpose to help and comfort we all!  you be go

to tell Uncle Oliver, and make he glad to see he, as you know you

be.'



'I know,' say James, 'that last time I be here, I behave ill enough

to make you dread my presence, Jane; but I have learn and suffer a

good deal since that time, and I wish for nothing so much as for my

uncle's pardon.'



Mrs. Beckett would have be more impressed, have she ever cease to

think of Master Jemmy otherwise than as a self-willed but candid boy;

and she answer as if he have be throw himself on her mercy after

break a window, or knock down Lord Fitzjocelyn--



'well, sir, that be all you can say.  I'm glad you be sorry.  I'll see

if I can mention, it to your uncle.'



off trot Jane, while Clara's indignation and excited spirit

relieve themselves by a burst of merry laughter, as she hang about her

brother, and beg to hear of the dear old home.



the old servant, in her simplicity, go straight upstairs, and up to

she nursling, as he have again become.  'Master Oliver,' say she, 'he

be come.  Master Jem be come back, and 'twould do your heart good to

see how happy the child be together--just like you and poor Master

Henry.'



'do she ask he here?' say Mr. Dynevor, uneasily.



'no, sir, he come right out of his own head, because he think she

would feel lost.'



Oliver vouchsafe no reply, and Jane press no far.  he never

allude to his guest; but when Clara come into the room, his eye dwelt

on her countenance of bright content and animation, and the smile that

play round her lip as she sit silent.  her voice be hush in the

sick-room, but he hear it about the house with the blithe, lively ring

CHAPTER XIX.



FAREWELL to GREATNESS.



  soon from the hall my father rear

  their scutcheon must descend.

                                   Scott





Mr. Holdsworth contrive to set James at liberty for a fortnight, and

cheerful habitation; but Clara's almost instant inquiry be for the
