as it use to be in old time, when she be the only fairy Disorder.

'however, I'll see to that,' quoth she to herself.  and she give

the new-comer--the eager conversation--Kitty thrust in her little

with Isabel carry off her little victim, one by one, to bed; and

James take the tea-tray down stair.  Clara follow with other part

of the equipage, and then both stand together warm themselves, and

gossip over the dear old kitchen fire, till Isabel come down and

find they there. and then, before any of the grand news be discuss,

all the infant marvel of the last fortnight have to be detail; and

the young parent require Clara's opinion whether they be spoil

Kitty.



next, Clara find her way to the cupboard, bring the shepherd and

shepherdess to light, look they well over, and satisfy herself that

certain that she do not kiss the damsel's delicate pink cheek--set

gaze at they till James accuse she of regard they as her penate!



'why, Jem!' she say, turn on he, 'you be a mere recreant if you

can feel it like home without they!'



'I have other porcelain figure to depend on for a home!' say James.



'take care, James!' say his wife, with the fond sadness of one whose

cup overflow with happiness; 'Clara's shepherdess may look fragile,

but she have keep her youth and see many a generation pass by of such

'she once be turn out of Cheveleigh, too, and have bear it as easily

Fanny.  there's a lady who, I warn you, can never withstand Fanny!'



Isabel take up her own defence, and they laugh on.  Poor Uncle

Oliver! could he but have know how little all this have to do with

Cheveleigh!









CHAPTER XX.



WESTERN TIDINGS.



  o lady! worthy of earth's proud throne!

  nor less, by excellence of nature, fit

  Beside an unambitious hearth to sit

  domestic queen, where grandeur be unknown--

  Queen and handmaid lowly.

                             WORDSWORTH.





a house in the Terrace be let, and the rent be welcome; and shortly

Salter, beg she to come as governess to her little brother,

Clara be much afraid that it be her duty to accept the proposal,

Miss Frost be not a person to be reject, and in another week she

Mr. Dynevor endure the step pretty well.  she have ascendancy enough

well endure.



peruvian mail be anticipate in the family with a feeling most akin

expect in a few week.'



Mr. Dynevor be oblige to resign himself; and so exceedingly fractious

'I congratulate you!'

of course she come; and she divine why, instead of at once enter

'that's right, don't be vex when it may be nothing.'

'you know well.'

thus it be, and there be happily many such friendship exist as

sometimes dangerous, it be because vanity, folly, and mistake be too

frequent; but in spite of all these, where woman be truly refine, and

exalt into companion and friend, there have be much more happy,

frank intercourse and real friendship than either the romantic or the

consciousness, no repining, and all be well.

     'I would not change my native land

      for rich Peru and all her gold;'

'then I should not give it to she.  pray write another, for she do

'I write to you for the last time, lest you should consider yourself

any long bind by the engagement which must long have be

colleague, you will know to what I allude, without my express any

present a fugitive from the hand of so-call justice; but I could

have bear all with a cheerful heart if you have not play I false.

you will never hear more of one who love you faithfully.



                                    'TH. MADISON.'





Poor Charlotte!  the wound be a great deal too deep for her usual

childish tear, or even for a single word.  she stand still, cold, and

yes, ma'am, that be a beautiful text you show I last night, I

think of it all day, and I'll try to believe that good will come out

of it.  I be sure you be very good to let I love the child!  I'm

certain sure Miss Salome know that I'm in trouble, for she never fail

to run and kiss I the minute she come in sight; and she'll sit so

quiet in my lap, the little dear, and look at I as much as to say,

'Charlotte, I wish I could comfort you.'  but it be all my own fault,

ma'am, and I think I could feel as if I be punish right, so I know

poor Tom be happy.'



'alas!' think Isabel, after hear Charlotte's reminiscence; 'how

close I have live to a world of which I be in utter ignorance!  how

little do we guess that, by the careless ease and inattention of our

household, we be carry about a firebrand, endanger not only

poor Walter, but do fearful harm wherever we go!'









CHAPTER XXI.



STEPPING WESTWARD.



   on Darien's sand and deadly dew.

                        Rokeby.





enterprise and speed both alike direct Fitzjocelyn's course across

the Isthmus of Panama, which in 1853 have newly become practicable for

adventurous traveller.  a canal conduct he as far as Cruces, after

which he have to push on through wild forest and swamp, under the escort

of the muleteer who take charge of the various traveller who have

arrive by the same packet.



it be a very novel and amusing journey, even in the very discomfort

and the strange character with whom he be throw, and more

discontent traveller use to declare that Don Luis, as he tell the

muleteer to call he, always seem to have the good success with the

surly hotel-keeper, though when he resign his acquisition to any

resolute grumbler, it use to be discover that he have be put up

with the bad share.



a place call Guallaval seem to be the most squalid and forlorn of

all the station--outside, an atmosphere of mosquito; inside, an

atmosphere of brandy and smoke, the master an ague-stricken Yankee, who

sit with his bare foot high against the wall, and only deign to jerk

with his head to show in what quarter be the drink and food, and to

'guess that stranger must sleep on the ground, for first-comer have

all the beds'--hammocks sling up in a barn, or unwholesome cupboard in

the wall.



at the dirty board sit several of the party first arrive, wash down

tough, stringy beef with brandy.  Louis be about to take his place

near a very black-bearded young man, who appear more civilized than

the rest, and who surprise he by at once make room for he, leave

the table with an air of courtesy; and when, in his halting Spanish, he

beg 'his grace' not to disturb himself, he be answer, in the same

tongue, 'I have finish.'



after the meal, such as it be, he wander out of the hut, to escape

the fume and the company within; but he be presently accost by the

same stranger, who, touch his slouch Panama hat, make he a speech

the man interrupt he in good English.  'these swamp breed fever, to

a certainty.  a cigar be the only protection; and even then there be

nothing more dangerous than to be out at sunset.'



'thank you, I be much oblige,' say Louis, turn towards the hut.

'have you be long out here?'



'the first time on the Isthmus; but I know these sort of place. pray

go in, my Lord.'



the title and the accent startled Louis, and he exclaim, 'you must be

from the Northwold country?'



he draw back, and say bluntly, 'never mind I, only keep out of this

pestiferous air.'



but the abrupt surliness complete the recognition, and, seize his

hand, Louis cry, 'Tom! how be you?'  you have turn into a thorough

Spaniard, and take I in entirely.'



'only come in, my Lord; I would never have speak to you, but that I

could not see you catch your death.'



'I be come: but what's the matter?  why avoid I, when you be the

very man I most wish to see?'



'I'm do for,' say Tom.  'the fellow up there have saddle their

roguery on I, and I'm off to the States.  I--'



'what do you say?  there, I be come in.  be satisfied, Tom; I be come

out with a commission from Mr. Dynevor, to see what can be arrange.'



'that's right,' cry Tom, 'now poor Miss Ponsonby will have one

friend.'



'your letter to Charlotte bring I out--' begin Louis; but Madison

break in with an expression of dismay and self-reproach at see he

walk somewhat lame.



'it be only when I be tired, and not think of it,' say Louis; 'do

you know that old ash stick, Tom, my constant friend?  see, here be

the name of all the place I have see cut out on it.'



'I know it, and you, the moment you sit down by the table,' say Tom,

in a tone of the utmost feeling, as Louis take his arm.  'you be not

one to forget.'



'and yet you be go to pass I without make yourself know.'



'a disgraced man have no business to be know,' say Tom, low and

hoarsely.  'no, I wish none of they ever to hear my name again; and but

for the slip of the tongue that come so naturally, you should not, but

I be draw to you, and could not help it.  I be glad I have see you

once more, my Lord--'



he would have leave he at the entrance, but Louis hold he fast.



'you be the very man I depend on for unravel the business.  a man

cannot be disgrace by any one but himself, and that be not the case

with you, Tom.'



'no, thank Heaven,' say Tom, fervently; 'I've keep my honesty, if I

have lose all the rest.'



little more be need to bring Madison to a seat on a wooden bench

and give he the alternative of share the spoil, or of be

would not believe I, my Lord, but I have almost a mind.  they would

take away my character, any way; and what advantage be my honesty

I do not; and such as that be think nothing of by very many.  I'd

get no faith in man nor woman leave, and I'd got nothing but suspicion

by my honesty; so why should I not give in to the way of the world, and

try if it would serve I.  but then, my Lord, it strike I that if I

have nothing else, I have still my God leave.'



Louis grasp his hand.



'yes, I'm thankful that Miss Ponsonby ask I to read to the Cornish

against God,' keep ring in my ear like a peal of bell, all night,

and by morning I send in a note to Mr. Robson, to say no to what he

propose.'



every other principle would have crack in such a conflict, and Louis

he have go back at once to his mine, in some hope that the threat

at the hope of establish his innocence; but declare that he should

then go at once to the States.--'What, not even go home to see

Charlotte?  I've get a letter for you, when I can get at it.'



Tom make no answer, and Fitzjocelyn fear that, in spite of all his

good quality, his fidelity in love have not equal his fidelity to

the poor little damsel's sweet blushing face and shy courtesy, and all

'the little hypocrite!  I don't want no more of her false word,'

'Hypocrite!  do you know how nobly and generously she have be help

Mr. and Mrs. Frost through their strait? how faithfully--'



'I know well,' say Tom, hoarsely; 'don't excuse she, my Lord; you

know little of what pass in your own kitchen.'



'too true, I fear, in many case,' say Louis; 'but I have see this

'no misunderstanding, my Lord.  I see, as plain as I see you, her name

and her write in the book that she give to Ford--her copy out of

of what he allege.'



and he have nearly throw the letter into the Pacific; but Louis catch

'do you ever read Cymbeline, Tom?'



'yes, to be sure I have,' growl Tom, in surprise.



'then remember Iachimo, and spare that letter.  what do he tell you?'

'and you could be deceive by such shallow coxcombry as this!' say

'I tell you there be the book,' return Tom.



'well, Tom, if Mr. Ford prove to be the Ford I take he to be, I'll

undertake that you shall see through he, and be heartily ashamed of

yourself.  give I back the letter,--you do not deserve to have it.'



'I don't want it,' say Tom, moodily; 'she have not be as true to I

as I've be to she, and if she ben't what I take she for, I do not

care to hear of she again.  I use to look at the mountain-top, and

think she be as pure as they; and that she should have be make

herself the talk of a fellow like that, and write so sweet to I all

the time!--No, my Lord, there's no excuse it; and 'twa her be

go after the rest that make it so bitter hard to I!  if she have be

true, I would have go through fire and water to be an honest man

worthy of she; but when I find how she have deceive I, it go hard

with I to cut myself off from the wild mountain life that I'd get to

love, and my poor nigger, that will hardly have so kind a master set

over they.'



'you have stand the fiery ordeal well,' say Louis; 'and I verily

believe that you will soon find that it be only an ordeal.'



the care of Tom be a wholesome distraction to the suspense that become

almost agony as Louis approach Peru, and behold the gigantic summit

of the more northern Andes, which sunset reveal shine out white and

fitfully, like the Pilgrim's vision of the Celestial City, although,

owe to their extreme distance, even on a bright noonday, nothing be

visible but clear deep-blue sky.  they seem to make he realize that

the decisive moment be near, when he should tread the same soil with

human hope and care seem to shrink into nothing before the eternity

and Infinite Greatness of which the depth and the height speak.  yet he

remembereth the hair of our head, who weigheth the mountain in the

balance, and counteth the isle as a very little thing.  Louis take

comfort, but nerve himself for resignation; his prayer be more, that

he might bear rightly whatever might be in store, than that he should

succeed.  he could hardly have make the latter petition with that

submissiveness and reserve befit all entreaty for blessing of this

pass world.









CHAPTER XXII.



rather SUDDEN.



  o! would you hear of a spanish lady,

    how she woo'd an Englishman?

  Garments gay, as rich as may be,

    deck with jewel she have on.

                         Old Ballad.





the white building of Callao look out of the palm gardens, and, with

until he should hear from he that evening or the next morning.



hire a calesa, he drive at once to Lima, to the house of the late Mr.

both rise; the one a well-dress man, the other--it be the well-know

visible, but what tone be ever like that low, distinct, earnest voice?

'Xavier should not--I will speak,' whisper her companion to she, and

all his doubt have return, and, in the crisis of his fate, he stand

the tear come drop from her eye, and then she could speak.

so Mary have find herself heiress to a share in the miserably-involve

affair of Dynevor and Ponsonby; and as soon as she could think of the

future at all, have form the design of settle Rosita in a convent

with a pension, and go herself to England.



but Rosita be not easily to be induce to give up her gaiety for a

convent life; and, moreover, there be absolutely such a want of ready

money, that Mary do not see how to get home, though Robson assure she

there be quite enough to live upon as they be at present.  nor be

it possible to dispose of the mine and other property without Mr.

Dynevor's consent, and he might not be in a state to give it.



the next stroke be young Madison's sudden disappearance, and the

declaration by Robson that he have carry off a great deal of

be profuse in compliment and attention, but continually defer the

he be talk fluently, and she be seek to rally her spirit, and

concern--it be then that Fitzjocelyn's voice be in her ear.



she have scarcely explain to Louis why his come be, if possible,

this be Lord Fitzjocelyn.'



Mr. Ward raise his eye to her face for one moment.  'I understand,'

he say, in a low, not quite steady voice.  'it be well.  will you

present I?' he add, as though collect himself like a brave man

after a blow.



'here be my kind friend,' she say, as she conduct he to Louis,

and they shake hand in the very manner she wish to see, learn

mutual esteem from her tone and each other's aspect.



'I be sorry to have intrude,' say Mr. Ward.  'I come in the hope that

you might find some mean of make I of use to you; and, perhaps, I

may yet be of some assistance to Lord Fitzjocelyn.'



he enforce the proposal with so much cordiality, and show so plainly

that it would be his chief pleasure and consolation to do anything for

Miss Ponsonby, that they do not scruple to take he into their

counsel; and Mary look on with exulting wonder at the ability and

readiness display by Louis in the discussion of business detail,

even with a man whose profession they be.  in remote space, almost

beyond memory, save to enhance the present joy of full reliance, be

the old uncomfortable sense of his lean too much upon she.  to have

he act and think for she, and with one touch carry off her

whole burthen of care, be comfort and gladness beyond what she have

even devise in imagination.  the only drawback, besides compassion for

Mr. Ward, be the shock of hearing of the extent of the treachery of

Robson, in whom her father have trust so implicitly, and to whom he

have show so much favour.



they agree that they would go to the Consul, and concert measure;

Mary only beg that Robson might not be hardly deal with, and they

become incredible as soon as Louis be out of sight.



by-and-by, he come back to the evening meal, when Rosita appear, with

her uncovered hair in two long, unadorned tress, plait, and hang

weight and coarseness, recall eastern fashion of mourning, which

Spain derive from the Moors.  she attempt a little spanish talk with

El Visconde, much to his inconvenience, though he be too joyous not to

be doubly good-natured, especially as he pity she, and regard she

as a very perplexing charge newly lay on he.



he have time to tell Mary that he be to sleep at the Consul's, whence

he have send a note and a messenger to fetch Tom Madison, since it

appear that the prosecution, the rumour of which have frighten the

and even if it have be, there be many under bad imputation at

large in the Peruvian Republic.



Fitzjocelyn have appoint that Robson should call on he early in the

morning, and, if he fail to detect he, intend to confront he with

Madison before the Consul, when there could be little doubt that his

guilt would be bring home to he.  he find that the Consul and Mr.

the amount of confidence repose in he; whereas Madison have be

remark as a young man of more than average intelligence and

steadiness, entirely free from that vice of gambling which be the bane

of all class in Spanish South America.  Mary sigh as she hear

learn what Rosita have squander.  next, he ask about the other

clerk, Ford, of whom Mary know very little, except that she have hear

Robson mention to her father, when prepare to set out for Guayaquil,

that in the consequent press of business he have engage a new

assistant, who have come from Rio as servant to a traveller.  she have

sometimes hear Robson speak in praise of his acquisition, and exalt

he above Madison; and once or twice she have see he, and fancy he

like some one whom she have know somewhere, but she have for many month

seldom leave her father's room, and know little of what pass beyond it.



Louis take his leave early, as he have to examine his prize, the

pocket-book, and make up his case before confront Robson; and he

tell Mary that he should refrain from see she on the morrow until

the 'tug of war should be over.'  'Mr. Ward promise to come to help

I,' he add.  'really, Mary, I never see a more generous or

considerate person.  I be constantly on the point of beg his

pardon.'



'I must thank he some way or other,' say Mary; 'his forbearance have

be beautiful.  I only wish he would have believe I, for I always

tell he the plain truth.  it would have spare he something; but

nobody would trust my account of you.'



the morning come, and with it Madison; but patient as Fitzjocelyn

mistake, and set off to call at the counting-house.



a black porter open the door, and he step forward into the inner

Fitzjocelyn be in too great haste to pause on this matter, and quickly

acknowledge the salutation, as if that of a stranger, demand where

Mr. Robson be.



in genuine surprise and alarm, Ford exclaim that he have not see he;

he think he be go to meet his lordship at the Consular residence.

no! could he be at his own house?  it be close by, and the question

be ask, but the Senor Robson have go out in the very early morning.

Ford look paler and paler, and while Louis say he would go and

to see if he have arrive there in the meantime.



Mary come to meet Louis in the sala, say that she be afraid that

they have not show sufficient consideration for poor Dona Rosita, who

really have feeling; she have go early to her convent, and have not yet

return, though she have be absent two hour.



Louis have but just explain his perplexity and vexation, when the old

Senora be come in, and excuse himself for having lose sight of she.

she have not go to the convent, but to the cathedral; and he, kneeling

her devotion, have suppose that she have go home unattended.



as he finish his story, there be a summon to Lord Fitzjocelyn to

I beg to state that he have the key of all the valuable at the office;

nothing be in my charge.'



Louis turn to consult Mary; but, as if a horrible idea have come over

Mary have more reason for her frightful suspicion than she would detain

he to hear.  Robson, always polite, have be especially so to the

more than once fancy that they be more at ease in her own absence.

she be certain that the saya y manto have be frequently employ to

enable Rosita to enjoy dissipation, when her husband's condition would

have render her public appearance impossible; and at the Opera or on

the Alameda, Robson might have have every opportunity of pay she

attention, and forward her amusement. there could be no doubt that

she have understand more of their plan than have be suppose, have

warn he, and share his flight.



Pursuit, capture, and a nunnery would be far great kindness to the

poor childish being, than leave she to the mercy of a runaway

swindler; and all measure be promptly take, Ford throw himself

into the chase with great ardour and indignation than even Madison;

for he have trust to Robson's grand profession that he could easily

mind since the Senora's warning, he have not think it necessary to

inform his confederate; and Ford be not only furious at the desertion,

but anxious to make a merit of his zeal, and encourage by having as

yet see no sign that he be recognise.



regardless of heat and fatigue, Fitzjocelyn, Mr. Ward, and the two

since out of harbour.  it be well that the pocket-book have be save,

for it contain security to a large amount, which Robson, after

show to Mary to satisfy she, doubtless intend to keep in hand for

such a start as the present.  without it, he have contrive, as Madison

know, to secure quite sufficient to remove any anxiety as to the

Senora Rosita own a fair share of her late husband's property.



the day of terrible anxiety make it a relief to Mary to have any

certainty, though she be infinitely shock at the tiding, which

apprehension for the poor fugitive herself, whom she love enough to

and religion present to she in a form that do nothing to make it

less childish.



Mary's first recovery from the blow be show by her remembering how

fatigued and heated Louis must be, and when she have give order for

will you do next, Mary!'



'I cannot tell.  Mrs. Willis and Mrs. ---- have both be ask I

very kindly to come to they, but I cannot let Mrs. Willis stay with I

away from her child.  yet it seem hard on Mr. Ward that you should

be come to I there.  I suppose I must go to Mrs. ----; but I wait

to consult you.  I have rather be at home, if it be right.'



'it may easily be make right,' quietly say Louis.



'how!' ask Mary.



'I find,' he continue, 'that the whole affair may be easily settle,

if you will give I authority.'



'I think I have give you authority to act in my name.'



'it might be simplify.'



'shall I sign my name!'



'yes--once--to make mine your.  if your claim be mine, I can take

much well care of the Dynevor interest.'



Mary rest her cheek on her hand, and look at he with her grave

steady face, not very much discompose after the first glimpse of his

'will you, Mary?'



'you know I will,' she say.



'then there be no time to be lose.  let it be to-morrow.  Yes'--going

on in the quiet deliberate tone that make it so difficult to interrupt

mine.  I find there be an offer that Robson keep secret. I could wind

up the account, see what can be save for the Northwold people, and

take you safe home by the end of a fortnight.'



'oh, Louis!' cry Mary, almost sob, 'this will not do.  I cannot

entangle you in our ruinous affair.'



'insufficient objection be consent,' say Louis, smile.  'do you

trust I, Mary?'



'it be of no use to ask.'



'you think I be not to be trust with affair that have become my own!

I believe I be, Mary.  you know I must do my utmost for the dynevor;

and I assure you I see my way.  I have no reasonable doubt of clear

off all future liability.  you mean to let I arrange?'



'yes, but--'



'then why not obviate all awkward situation at once?'



'my father!  you should not ask it, Louis.'



'I would not hasten you, but for the sake of my own father, Mary.  he

be grow old, and I could not have leave he for anything but the hope

of bring he his own choose daughter.  I want you to help I take

care of he, and we must not leave he alone to the long evening and

cold wind.'



Mary be yielding--'I must not keep you from he,' she say, 'but

to-morrow--a Sunday, too--'



'ah!  Mary, do you want gaiety!  no, if we cannot have it in a holy

place, let it at least have the consecration of the day--let we have

fifty-two wedding day a year instead of one.  indeed, I would not

press you, but that I could take care of you so much well, and it be

year, I think?'



'settle it as you like,' she manage to say, with a great flood of

tear-but what soft bright tear!  'I trust you.'



he see she want solitude; he only stay for a few word of earnest

the arrangement.  and there be one thing I must do first, one poor

fellow who must not be leave in suspense any long.'



tired as he ought to have be, he lightly cross the sala to the room

appropriate to business, where he have desire the two clerk to wait

arm, while Ford lounge in a disengage attitude on a chair, but rise

alert and respectful at his appearance.



Louis ask one or two necessary question on the custody of the office

for the night and ensue day, and Ford make repeat assurance that

nothing would be find missing that have be leave in his charge.  'I

believe you, Mr. Delaford,' say Fitzjocelyn, quietly.  'I do not think

the low specie of fraud be ever in your style.'



Delaford try to open his lip, but visibly shake.  Louis answer,

what he have not yet say, 'I do not intend to expose you.  I think you

have what excuse neglect can give, and unless I should be call on

conscientiously to speak to your character, I shall leave you to make a

new one.'



Delaford begin to stammer out thank, and promise of explain the

whole of Robson's peculation (little he know the whole of they).



'there be one earnest of your return to sincerity that I require,' say

Louis.  'explain at once the degree of your acquaintance with Charlotte

Arnold.'



Tom Madison still stand moody--affect not to hear.



'oh! my Lord, I do not know that you be interested in that young

person.'



'I be interested where innocence have be malign,' say Louis,

sternly.



'I be sure, my Lord, nothing have ever pass at which the most

particular need take umbrage,' exclaim Delaford.  'if Mr. Madison

will recollect, I mention nothing as the most fastidious need--'



Mr. Madison would not hear.



'you only infer that she have not be insensible to your

attraction?'



'why, indeed, my Lord, I flatter myself that in my time I have have the

happiness of not be unpleasing to the sex,' say Delaford, with a

sigh and a simper.



'it be a mortifying question, but you owe it to the young woman to

answer, whether she give you any encouragement.'



'no, my Lord.  I must confess that she always speak of a previous

attachment, and dash my early hope to the ground.'



'and the book of poem!  how come that to be in your possession?



Delaford confess that it have be a little tribute, return upon his

hand by the young lady in question.



'one question more, Mr. Delaford: what be the fact as to her lending

you mean for your voyage?'



Delaford be not easily bring to confession on this head; but he do

at length own that he have go in great distress to Charlotte, and have

appeal to her bounty; but he distinctly acknowledge that it be not

in the capacity of suitor; in fact, as he end by declare, he have

the pleasure of say that there be no young person whom he esteem

more highly than Miss Arnold, and that she have never give he the

least encouragement, such as need distress the happy man who have

secure her affection.



the happy man do not move till Delaford have leave the room, when Louis

walk up to he and say, 'I can far tell you, of my own

knowledge, that that good girl refuse large wage, and a lady's-maid's

place, partly because she would not live in the same house with that

man; and she have work on with a faithful affection and constancy,

beyond all praise, as the single servant to Mr. and Mrs. Frost in their

distress.'



'don't talk to I, my Lord,' cry Tom, turn away; 'I'm the most

unhappy man in the world!'



'I do not ask you to shake hand with Delaford to-night.  you will

another day.  he be only a vain coxcomb, and treat you to a little of

his conceit, with, perhaps, a taste of spite at a successful rival; but

he have only show you what a possession you have in she.'



'you don't know what I've do, my Lord.  I have write she a letter

that she can never forgive!'



'you don't know what I've do, Tom.  I post a letter by the mail

just start from Callao--a letter to Mr. Frost, with a hint to

Charlotte that you be labour under a little delusion; I know, from

your first narration, that Ford could be no other than my old friend,

shorn of his beam.'



'that letter--' still mutter Tom.



'she'll forgive, and like you all the well for having afford she a

catastrophe, Tom.  you may write by the next mail; unless, what be

well still, you come home with we by the same, and speak for

yourself.  if I be your master then, I'll give you the holiday.  yes,

Tom, it be important to I to clear up your countenance, for I want to

bespeak your service to-morrow as my friend.'



'my Lord!' cry Tom, aghast.  'if you do require any such service,

though I should not have think it, there be many nearer your own

rank, officer and gentleman fitter for an affair of the kind.  I never

know anything about fire-arm, since I give up poach.'



'indeed, Tom, I be very far from intend to dispense with your

service.  I want you to guide I to procure the require weapon!'



'surely,' say Tom, with a deep, reluctant sigh, 'you never cross the

Isthmus without one?'



'yes, indeed, I do; I never see the party there whom I should have

like to challenge in this way.  why, Tom, do you really think I have

come out to Peru to fight a duel on a Sunday morning?'



'that's what come of live in this sort of place.  duel be meat and

drink to the people here,' say Tom, ashamed and relieve, 'and there

have be those who tell I it be all that be want to make I a

gentleman.  but in what capacity be I to serve you, my Lord!'



'in the first place, tell I where I may procure a wedding-ring! yes,

Tom, that's the weapon!   you've no objection to be my friend in

that capacity!'



Tom's astonished delight go beyond the bound of expression, and

therefore be compress into an almost grim 'whatever you will, my

Lord;' but two hot tear be gush from his eye.  he dash they

away, and add, 'what a fool I be!  you'll believe I, my Lord, though

I cann't speak, that, though there may be many near and more your

equal, there's none on earth more glad and happy to see you so, than

myself.'



'I believe it, indeed, Tom; shake hand, to wish I joy; I be right

glad to have one here from Ormersfield, to make it more home-like. for,

though it be a hurry at last, you can guess what she have be to I

from the first.  know she thoroughly have be one of the many, many

benefit that Ferny dell confer on I.'



there be no time for more than to enjoin silence.  Louis have to hurry

to the Consul and the Chaplain, and to overcome their astonishment.



on the other hand, Mary be, as usual, seek and recover the

balance of her startled spirit in her own chamber.  she see the matter

wisely and simply, and have full confidence in Louis, with such a

yearning for his protection that, it may be, the strange suddenness of

the proposal cost she the less.  she come forth and announce she

intention to Mrs. Willis, who be inclined to resent it as derogatory

to the dignity of womanhood, and the privilege of a bride; but Mary

smile and answer that, 'when he have take so much trouble for she,

she could not give he any more by thing of that sort.  she must be as

little in his way as possible.'



and Mrs. Willis sigh, and pity she, but be glad that she should be

off her poor brother's mind as soon as might be, and be glad to resign

her task of chaperone she.



only three person beyond the Consul's family know what be about to

happen, when Miss Ponsonby, in her deep mourning, attend the morning

service in the large hall at the Consul-house; and such eye as be

direct towards the handsome stranger, only gaze at the unwonted

spectacle of an english nobleman, not with the more eager curiosity

that would have be attach to he have all be know.



Mr. Ward linger a few moment, and beg for one word with Miss

Ponsonby. she could not but comply, and come to meet he, blushing, but

compose, in that simple, frank kindness which only wish to soften

the disappointment.



'Mary,' he say, 'I be not come to harass you.  I have do so long

enough, and I would not have torment you, but on that one head I do

not do justice to your judgment.  I see now how vain my hope be. I be

glad to have meet he--I be glad to know how worthy of you he be, and to

have see you in such hand.'



'you be very kind to speak so,' say Mary.



'yes, Mary, I could not have bear to part with you, if I be not

convince that he be a good man as well as an able man.  I might have

know that you would not choose otherwise.  I shall see your name among

the great lady of the land.  I come to say something else.  I wish

to thank you for the many happy hour I have spend with you, though you

never for a moment trifle with I.  it be I who deceive myself.

good-bye, Mary.  perhaps you will write to my sister, and let she know

of your arrival.'



'I will write to you, if you please,' say Mary.



'it will be a great pleasure,' he say, earnestly.  'and will you let

I be of any use in my power to you and Lord Fitzjocelyn?'



'indeed, we shall be most grateful.   you have be a most kind and

forbearing friend.  I should like to know that you be happy,' say

Mary, lingering, and hardly know what to say.



'my little niece be fond enough of their uncle.  my sister want I.

in short, you need not vex yourself about I.  some day, when I be an

old man, I may come and bring you news of Lima.  meanwhile, you will

sometimes wear this bracelet, and remember that you have an old friend.

I shall call on Lord Fitzjocelyn at the office to-morrow, and see if we

can find any clue to Robson's retreat.  good-bye, and blessing on you,

Mary.'



Mary rejoin Louis, to speak to he of the kind and noble man who so

generously and resolutely bear the wreck of his hope.  they walk up

and down together in the cool shade of the tree in the Consul's

garden, and they speak of the unselfishness which seem to take away

the smart from the wound of disappointment.  they speak sometimes, but

the day be for the most part spend in the sweetness of pensive, happy

silence, muse with full heart over this crowning of their long

defer hope, and not without prayer that the same protect Hand

might guide they, as they should walk together through life.



by-and-by Mary disappear.  she would perhaps have prefer she

ordinary dress--but the bridal white seem to she to be due both to

Louis and to the solemn rite and mystery; and when the time come, she

meet he, in her plain white muslin and long veil, confine by a few

spray of real orange flower, beneath which her calmly noble face be

see, simple and collect as ever, forget in her earnestness all

adjunct that might have be embarrassing or distressing.



the large hall be darken with twilight, and the flower and

branch that deck it show gracefully in the subdue light. prayer

and praise have lately echo there, and Louis and Mary could feel that

he be with they who bless the pair at Cana, far distant as they be

from their own church--their own home.  yes, the Church, their mother,

their home, be with they in her sacred ritual and her choice

blessing, and their conscience be free from self-will, or

self-pleasing, such as would have put far from they the precious gift

promise in the name of their Lord.



when it be over, and they first raise their eye to one another's

face, each beheld in the other a look of entire thankful content, not

the less perfect because it be grave and peaceful.



'I think mamma would be quite happy,' say Mary.









CHAPTER XXIII.



the MARVEL of PERU.



  Turn, Angelina, ever dear,

    my charmer, turn to see

  Thy own, thy long-lose Edwin here,

    restore to love and thee,

                              goldsmith.





Lord Ormersfield sit alone in the library, where the fire burn more

for the sake of cheerfulness than of warmth.  his eye be weary with

reading, and, take off his spectacle, he turn his chair away from

the table, and sit gaze into the fire, give audience to dreamy

thought.



he miss the sunny face ever prompt to watch his mood, and find or

make time for the cheerful word or desultory chat which often break and

refresh dry occupation.  he remember when he have hardly tolerate

the glass of flower, the scrap of drawing, the unbusinesslike book

at his son's end of the table, but the room look dull without they

now, and he be ready to own the value of the grace and finish of life,

hinder the daily task from absorb the whole man, as have be the

case with himself in middle life.



somewhat of the calm of old age have begin to fall on the Earl, and he

have latterly be willnot to think more deeply.  these trifle could not

have speak to his heart save for their connexion with his son, and

even Louis's taste would have wear out with habit, have it not be for

the radiance permanent in his own mind, namely, the thankful, adoring

love that find the true brightness in "whatsoever thing be pure,

whatsoever thing be lovely, whatsoever thing be of good report."

this spirit it be which have keep his heart fresh, his spirit youthful,

and change constitutional versatility into a power of hearty

adaptation to the least congenial taste.



Gentleness, affection, humility, and refinement be in his nature.

Mrs. Frost have train these quality into the beauty of Christian

grace; and Mrs. Ponsonby and her daughter have teach he to bring his

high principle to supply that which be want.  Indolence of will,

facility of disposition, unsteadiness of purpose, inconsiderate

impulse without perseverance, have all betoken an inherent weakness,

which the Earl's cure, ambition, have be powerless to remedy; but duty

have be effectual in draw strength out of what have be feeble by

nature.  it be religion that have make a man of Louis; and his father

see and own it, no long as merely the woman's guide in life and the

man's resource chiefly in death, to be respect and moderately

attend to, but never so as to interfere unreasonably with the world.

no; he have learn that it be the only sure and sound move-spring: he

know it as his son's strengthening, brighten thread of life; and

begin to perceive that his own course might have be less gloomy and

less harsh, devoid of such dark strand, have he hold the right clue.

the contrast bring back some line which, without mark, he have

hear Louis and his aunt read together, and, albeit little willnot to

look into his son's book, he be so much haunted by the rhythm that he

rise and search they out--



          Yea, mark he well, ye cold and proud,

          Bewildered in a heartless crowd,

            start and turn pale

              at rumour's angry din:

            no storm can now assail

              the charm he bear within.

          rejoice still, and do good,

          and with the thought of God imbue,

            no glare of high estate,

              no gloom of woe or want,

            the radiance may abate,

              where Heaven delight to haunt.





the description go to his heart, so well do it agree with Louis. yet

there be a sad feeling, for the south american mail have be some day

due, and he have not hear of his son since he be about to land at

Callao.  five month be a long absence; and as the chance of failure,

disappointment, climate, disease, and shipwreck arise before he, he

marvel at himself for having consent to peril his sole treasure,

and even fancy that a solitary, childless old age might be the

penalty in store for having wait to be lead heavenward by his son.



it be seldom that the Earl give way, and, reproach himself for his

weakness, he rouse himself and rang the bell for well light. there

be a movement in the house, and for some moment the bell be not

answer; but presently the door be open.



'bring the other lamp.'



'yes, my Lord.'



the slow, soft voice do not belong to Frampton.  he start up, and

there stand Louis!



'my dear father,' he say; and Lord Ormersfield spring up, grasp his

son's hand, and lay the other hand on his shoulder, but durst ask no

'Lady Fitzjocelyn!'



and that very moment Mary be in Lord Ormersfield's arm.

all I ever wish!'



he hold a hand of each, and look from one to the other till Mary

'you will forgive our not write?  we land this morning, find the

'no, indeed,' say his father; 'if all surprise be like this one!

but you be the loser, Mary.  I be afraid this be not the reception for

'Mary have dispense with much that belong to a bride,' say Louis.

'see here!' and, seize her hand, he begin pull off her glove, till

she do it for he; 'do you ever see such a wedding-ring?--a great,

solid thing of peruvian gold, with a spanish posy inside!'



'I like it,' say Mary; 'it shows--'



'what you be worth, eh, Mary?  well! here we be!  it seem real at

last!  and you, father, have you be well?'



'yes, well indeed, now I have you both!  but how come you so quickly?

you never bring she across the Isthmus?'



'indeed I do.  she would come.  it be her first act of rebellion; for

we be not go to let you meet the frost alone--the October frost,

I mean; I hope the Dynevor frost be all right?'



Frampton be here see at the open door, doubtful whether to intrude;

yet, impel by necessity, as he catch Fitzjocelyn's eye, he,

hesitating, said--



'my Lord, the spanish gentleman!'



'the great triumph of my life!' cry Louis, actually clap his

'why, Frampton, don't you know he?'



'my Lord!!!'



'let I introduce you, then, to--Mr. Thomas Madison!' and, as Frampton

'Young Madison, my Lord!' repeat Frampton, slowly come to his

'no other.  he have do Lady Fitzjocelyn and all of we infinite

'he be to stay here for a short time before go to his appointment at

'and poor Delaford's witness be quite as much to his credit,' say

'ay! if Delaford have not be equally willing to depose against he

when he be the apparent catiline!' say Louis.  'Poor Delaford! he be

well fate than go off to the digging with a year's salary in his

(Footnote.  a recent writer relate that he find the near relation of

a nobleman gain a scanty livelihood as shoe-black at the digging.

Query.  might not this be Mr. Delaford?)



'then everything be settle?' ask his father.



'almost everything.  the mine be off our hand, and the transfer will

be complete as soon as Oliver have send his signature; and there's

quite enough save to make they very comfortable.  you have tell I

nothing of they yet?'

'pray, how do Jem bear it?'



'with tolerable equanimity.  it may be many year before the child be

a wise man than he use to be.'

'yes, Mr. Calcott say that he live in fear of some one offer he a

living,' say Lord Ormersfield.



'and the dear old Giraffe?' say Louis.



'Clara?  she be look almost handsome.  I wish some good man would

'I be not ready to spare she yet,' say Mary; 'I must make acquaintance

with she before any excellent man carry she off.'



'but there be a marriage that will surprise you,' say the Earl; 'your

eld cousin, whose name I can never remember--'



'Virginia,' cry Louis.  'Captain Lonsdale, I hope!'



'what could have make you fix on he?'



'because the barricade could not have be in vain, and he be an

'you remember that Lady Conway would not take our advice, and stay

'my poor aunt!  no one ever pity she when she be 'hoist with her own

petard!'  I be glad poor Virginia be to be happy in her own way.'



'I shall send my congratulation to-morrow,' say the Earl, smile

triumphantly, 'and a piece of intelligence of my own.  at H. B. M.

Consul's, Lima--what day be it, Louis?'



Mary run away to take off her bonnet, as much surprise by the Earl's

the answer excite a peal of laughter, which make Louis stand aghast,

Louis briefly explain that, the poor lady be provide for by

'I be sure I feel so,' say Mary.  'you know this always seem more

'I'm afraid I cannot,' say Mary, look up in his face with an arch,

deprecate expression.



'why not?'



'don't you know that I be so much happy?'



before breakfast next morning Fitzjocelyn must visit his farm, and Mary

must come with he.



how delicious be that english morning after their voyage; the slant

ray of the sun silver the turf, and cast rainbow across the

gossamer thread from one brown bent to another; the harvest field on

the slope dot with rich sheaf of wheat; the coppice, in their

summer glory, here and there touch with the gold of early autumn, and

the slope and meadow bright with lively green, a pleasant change for

eye fresh from the bare, rugged mountain-side and the rank unwholesome

vegetation of Panama.  Shaggy little scottish oxen be feed on the

dewy grass, their black coat look sleek in the sun beyond the long

shadow of the thorn; but as Mary say, laugh, 'only Farmer

Fitzjocelyn's cattle come here now,' and she stop more than once to

be introduce to some notable animal, or to hear the history of

experiment in fat beast.



'there! they have find you out!  that's for you,' say Louis, as a

up through the tranquil air.  'yes, Ormersfield, you be greet a

friend!  you may be very glad, old place!  I wish Mr. Holdsworth would

come up to breakfast!  be it too wet for you this way, Mary?'



this way be into Fernydell, and Mary answer, 'oh, no--no; it be

where I most want to go with you.  we have never be there together

'no, you never would walk with I after I could go alone!' say Louis,

with a playful tone of reproach, veil deep feeling.



in silence he hand she down the rocky step, plunge deeply among

both by the verdure and the form of the rock, the letters--



                  Deo Gratias,

                   L. F. 1847.





'I like that!' be all that Mary's full heart allow she to say.



'yes,' say Louis, 'I feel quite as thankful for the accident as for

the preservation.'



'and that dear mamma be with we,' add Mary.  'between she and you,

have cut they out long ago.'



'as soon as I could get here without help,' he answer.  'I think I

they be, with the fruit just form and blush.'



'like other thing,' say Mary, reach after the spray, 'once all

blossom, now--'

'remember it!' say Mary.  'I don't think I forget a day of that

'what I bring you here for,' say Louis, 'be to ask you to let I do

it be the spanish gentleman, redden all over at such an encounter,

about half an hour after, Clara be delicately blow the dust out of

'and Louis be come, and Mary!  oh! isabel, he have she at last!  oh!

Jem! Jem! do we ever want dear granny so much!  I always know it would

come right at last!  Jane, Jane, do you hear, Lord Fitzjocelyn be

married!  let I in; I must go and tell Uncle Oliver!'



James look at Isabel, and read in her smile Clara's final acquittal

Lord Ormersfield could have thank she for that joyous, innocent

even the Earl be glad to escape to the sitting-room, though every one

'and what be his name, Kitty?'



'Woland!' shout all the young lady in chorus.



'Sir Woland be in the book that mamma do make,' say Kitty.



Louis look at Isabel with laugh eye.



'it be Uncle Oliver's great wish,' she say, 'and we do not wish to

remember the day of Sir Hubert.'



before Lord Ormersfield be quite deafen, Louis recollect that they

'I hear you have be in hysteric again,' have be Lord Fitzjocelyn's

Charlotte be prepare.  the mutual pardon have not be very hard to

Mrs. Beckett make he exceedingly welcome, and he spend the chief part

of his time at No. 5, where he be much more at ease than at

Ormersfield.  he confess that, though not give to bashfulness before

any man, there be something in Mr. Frampton's excessive civility that

quite overcome he, and make he always expect to be kick out of

door the next minute for sauciness.



Charlotte's whirlwind of feeling have nearly expend themselves in

that one shock of meeting.  the year of cheerful toil, and the week

heart, and the prospect of her new duty bring on she a sobering

sense of responsibility.  she would always be tender and cling, but

the fragrant woodbine would be train round a sound, sturdy oak, and

her modesty, gentleness, and sincerity, give every promise of her be

an excellent wife.



Tom have little time to spare before undertake his new office, and it

be well that the parting should be speedy, for it be a grievous

Clara's first outlay, on her restoration to comparative wealth, be on

with Fitzjocelyn, she go to London for one day, to put the final

good old Miss Ponsonby's heart be some degree soft and less narrow

the Earl be bent on rejoicing for his son's marriage, and Louis

slave of all the world, work as hard as Mary and Louis at all the

event, less bright than the previous toil.



the wedding take place in Ormersfield Church, on a bright September

morning; James Frost perform the marriage, Lord Fitzjocelyn give the

bride away, and little Kitty be the bridesmaid.  the ring be of

peruvian gold, and the brooch that clasp the bride's lace collar be

dove-colour silk, she look as simple and ladylike as she be

pretty, and a very graceful contrast to her spanish gentleman

bridegroom.



the Ormersfield bowling-green, which be willnot to be so still and

desert, hem in by the dark ilex belt, beheld such a scene as have

not take place there since its present master be a boy.  there be

long table spread for guest of all rank and degree.  Louis have his

Sir Miles Oakstead have come to see his old friend make happy, and to

James Frost long since forget; and Sir Gilbert Brewster shine in his

colonel's uniform,--for Lady Fitzjocelyn have intimate a special desire

belief that her own peculiar hero look in his blue and silver 'as

well as Lord Fitzjocelyn himself.'  and Miss Mercy Faithful be there,

watch over Oliver, to make up for the want of her sister.  and old

there, and everywhere--now set Mrs. Richardson at ease, now

she to mingle with group after group of people, and hear from one and

another how handsome and how happy Lord Fitzjocelyn look, and Lady

Fitzjocelyn quite beautiful; and, then, as they walk from party to

party, set all at ease and leave pleased look wherever they

go, to cross they now and then, and exchange a blithe smile or merry

remark.



no melancholy gap here! think she, as she help her uncle to the

easy chair prepare for he at the dinner-table; no spiritless

curiosity, no force attempt to display what no one feel!



there must need be toast, and such as think themselves assemble

for the sake of the 'marriage in high life,' be take by surprise

when Lord Fitzjocelyn rise, and begin by thank those assemble for

assist in do honour to the event of the day--the marriage of two

person, for each of whom he himself as well as those most dear to he

feel the warm respect and gratitude for essential service and

disinterested attachment, alike in adversity and in prosperity.

unpleasant as he know it be to have such truth speak to one's face,

he could not deny himself the satisfaction of express a portion of

the esteem and reverence he feel for such noble conduct as have be

display by those whose health he have the pleasure to propose--Mr. and

Mrs. Thomas Madison.



'there,' be his aside, as he sit down, 'I only hope I have not make

he surly; poor fellow, I have put he in a predicament, but it could

not be otherwise!'



Clara have tear in her eye, but not like those she have shed at

Cheveleigh; James give Louis a look of heartfelt gratitude, bow the

low to the happy pair, and hold up little Kitty that her imitative

nod and sip might not be lose upon they.



Mrs. Beckett say, 'well, I never!  if ever a girl deserve it,'

choke, and flourish her white handkerchief; Frampton salute like my

Lord and Louis XIV. roll into one; and Warren and Gervas privately

agree that they do not know what be come of the world, since

Marksedge poacher have only to go to foreign part to be coin goold

in the silver mine.  Mrs. Madison's pretty face be all blush,

smile, and tear.  Mr. Madison rise to reply with unexpected alacrity,

and Louis be soon relieve from anxiety, at least, as far as regard

his eloquence, for he think in the majestic spanish idiom, and

translate as he went--



'my Lords,' he begin, 'gentleman and lady and neighbour, my Lord

Fitzjocelyn have do my wife and myself an honour as unlooke-for as

undeserved; and the manner of the favour be such that we shall carry

the grateful remembrance to the end of our life.  he have be so

condescend as to speak of such service as it be in our power to

render; but he have pass over in silence that which give he a claim

to the utmost that I could place at his foot.  he will forgive I for

speak openly, for I cannot refrain from disburthene my mind, and

let you know, even more than you be at present aware of, what your

Senor--what your Lord truly be.  Most of you have know I but too

well.  it be not ten year since I be a rude, untaught boy upon the

heath, such as a large proportion of those present would deem beneath

their notice: Lord Fitzjocelyn do not think so.  his kindness of

manner and encourage word awaken in I new life and energy.  he

give I his time and his teaching, and, what be far more, he give I

his sympathy and his example.  it be these which give vitality to

lesson dimly understand, or which have fall dead on my ear, when

only hear in my irregular attendance at school. but the work in I be

tardy, and at first I requite his kindness with presumption,

insubordination, and carelessness.  then, when I have be dismiss,

and when my wilful neglect have occasion the accident of which the

trace be still only too visible, then, do I not merit to be expose

and cast off for ever?  I know it, and I flee, as if I could leave

behind I my grief and my shame.  little do I dare to guess that he

be deal with I as though I have be his own brother, and

scrupulously conceal my share in the misfortune.  when I return,

sullen and overwhelmed, he alone--yes! and while still suffer

severely--speak a kind word to I, and exert himself to rescue I

from the utter ruin and degradation to which despair would have lead I.

he place I in the situation which conduct I to my present

position; he give I the impulse to improve myself; and, above all, he

infuse into I the principle without which the rest would have be

mere temptation.  if I have be blest beyond my desert--if I have

be prosperous beyond reasonable expectation--if, among numerous

failure, I have withstand some evil--all, under the great and

high Benefactor, be owe to the kindness, and, above all, to the

generous forbearance of Lord Fitzjocelyn.  I wish I could testify my

gratitude in any well manner than by speak of he to his face; but

I be sure you will all drink his health more heartily, if possible, for

know one more trait in addition to your own personal experience of

his character!'



Alas! that all thing hide, and yet to be proclaim on the

house-top, would bear the light as well as Fitzjocelyn's secret!  the

revelation of this unobtrusive act of patience and forbearance excite

a perfect tumult of enthusiasm among person already work up to great

ardour for one so beloved; and shout, and even tear, on every side

strove in vain to express the response to Madison's word.



'too bad, Tom!' be Louis's muttered comment.



'you be pay in your own coin,' retort Mary, raise her glistening

eye, full of archness.



'I perceive it be no surprise to you, Lady Fitzjocelyn!' say Sir Miles

oakstead; 'and, I own, nothing from that quarter' (nod at Louis)

'surprise I greatly.'



'she practise eavesdropping,' say Louis, 'when the poor fellow be

relieve his mind by a confession to the present Mrs. Madison.'



'and I think Mrs. Madison and I deserve credit for having keep the

secret so long,' say Mary.



'it explain,' observe Mr. Holdsworth.  'I do not understand your

power over Madison.'



'it be the making of we both,' say Louis; 'and a very fine speciman

of the grandeur of that rough diamond.  it elucidate what I have

always say, that if you can but find the one vulnerable place, there

be a wonderful fund of nobleness in some of these people.'



'do you take this gentleman as an average speciman?'



'every ploughboy be not an undeveloped Madison; but in every parish

there may be some one with either the _think_ or the rise element

in his composition; and if the right ingredient be not add, the

fermentation will turn sour, as my neglect have very nearly make it do

with he.  he would have be a fine demagogue by this time, if he have

not have a generous temper and Sunday-school foundation.'



'hush!' say Mary, smiling--'you must not moralize.  I believe you be

do it that poor Farmer Norris may not catch your eye.'



Louis give a debonnaire glance of resignation; and the farmer, rise

in the full current of feeling cause by Madison's speech, say, with

thorough downright emotion, that he know it be of no use to try to

enhance what have be already so well express, but he believe there

be scarcely a person present who do not feel, equally with Mr.

Madison, the right to claim Lord Fitzjocelyn as a personal friend,--and

an irrepressible hum of fervent assent prove how truly the farmer

speak.  'Yes,--each have in turn experience so much of his friendly

kindness, and, what be more, of his sympathy, that he could

confidently affirm that there be scarcely one in the neighbourhood who

have not learn the news of his happiness as if some good thing have

happen to himself individually.  they all as one man be delighted

to have he at home again, and to wish he joy of the lady, whom many

of they know already well enough to rejoice in welcome she for her

own sake, as well as for that of Lord Fitzjocelyn.'



again and again do the cheer break forth--hearty, homely, and

sincere; and such be the bright, tearful, love eye, which seek

those of Fitzjocelyn on every side, that his own fill so fast that

all seem dazzle and misty, and he hastily strove to clear they as he

arise; but the swelling of his heart bring the happy dew again, and

would scarcely let he find voice.  'my friend, my dear, good friend,

you be all very kind to I.  it be of no use to tell you how little I

deserve it, but you know how much I wish to do so, and here be one who

have help I, and who will help I.  we thank you with all our heart.

you may well wish my father and I joy, and yourself too.  thank you;

you should not look at I so kindly if you wish I to say more.'



the Earl, who have study popularity as a useful engine, but have never

prize love beyond his own family, be exceedingly touch by the

ardour of enthusiastic affection that his son have obtained,--not by

courting suffrage, not by gift, not by promise, but simply by real

open-hearted love to every one.  Lord Ormersfield himself come in for

demonstration of warm feeling which he would certainly never have

seek nor obtain ten year ago, when he be respect and look up

to as an upright representative of certain opinion; but personally,

either dislike or regard with coldness.



he know what these cheer be worth, and that even Fitzjocelyn might

not long be the popular hero; but he be not the less gratified and

triumphant, and feel that no success of his whole life have be worth

the present.



'after all, Clara,' say Oliver Dynevor, as his nephew and niece be

assist he to the carriage, 'they have manage these thing well

than we do, though they do not have Gunter.'



'gunter cann't bring heart's love down from town in a box,' say Clara,

in a flash of indignation.  'no, dear uncle, there be thing that

cann't be get unless by live for they.'



'nor even by live for they, Clara,' say James; 'you must live for

something else.'



Lord Ormersfield have hear these few last word, and there be deep

thought in his eye as he bid his cousin farewell at the hall door.



Clara be the last to take her place; and, as she turn round with a

merry smile to wish he goodbye, he say, 'you have be make

yourself very useful, Clara, I be afraid you have have no time to enjoy

yourself.'



'that's a contradiction,' say Clara, laugh; 'here's busy little

Kitty, who never be thoroughly happy but when she think she be useful,

and I be child enough to be of the same mind.  I never be unhappy but

when I be set to enjoy myself.  it have be the most beautiful day of

my life.  thank you for it.  goodbye!'



the Earl cross the hall, and find Mary stand alone on the terrace

step, look out at the curl smoke from the cottage chimney, and

on the coppice and hedge-row.



'be you tired, my dear?' he say.



'oh no!  I be only think of dear mamma's persuade Louis to go on

with the crumpled plan of those cottage.  how happy she would be.'



'I be think of she likewise,' say the Earl.  'she speak truly when

she tell I that he might not be what I then wish to make he, but

something far well.'



Mary look up with a satisfied smile of approval, say, 'I be so

glad you think so.'



'yes,' say Lord Ormersfield, 'I have think a good deal since.  I

have be alone here, and I think I see why Louis have do well than

some of his elder.  it seem to I that some of we have not know the

duty that lie by the way-side, so to speak, from the main purpose of

life.  I wish I could talk it over with your mother, my dear, what do

you think she would say?'



Mary think of Louis's vision of the thread.  'I think,' she say,

'that I have hear she say something like it.  the real aim of life be

out of sight, and even good people be too apt to attach themselves to

what be tangible, like friendship or family affection, or usefulness,

or public spirit; but these be like the path of glory which lead but

to the grave, and no far.  it be the single-hearted, faithful aim

towards the one thing needful, to which all other thing may be add

as mere accessory.  it bring down strength and wisdom.  it bring

the life everlaste already to begin in this life, and so make the

path shine more and more unto the perfect day!'









the END.
